<li class="nv-edit"><b><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:FDRSeries&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></b></li>

<p><b>Franklin Delano Roosevelt</b> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English" title="Help:IPA for English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/?/ primary stress follows">?</span><span title="'r' in 'rye'">r</span><span title="/o?/ long 'o' in 'code'">o?</span><span title="'z' in 'Zion'">z</span><span title="/?/ 'a' in 'about'">?</span><span title="'v' in 'vie'">v</span><span title="/?/ 'a' in 'about'">?</span><span title="'l' in 'lie'">l</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span></span>/</a></span></span>, his own pronunciation,<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1"><span>[</span>1<span>]</span></a></sup> or <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English" title="Help:IPA for English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/?/ primary stress follows">?</span><span title="'r' in 'rye'">r</span><span title="/o?/ long 'o' in 'code'">o?</span><span title="'z' in 'Zion'">z</span><span title="/?/ 'a' in 'about'">?</span><span title="'v' in 'vie'">v</span><span title="/?/ short 'e' in 'bed'">?</span><span title="'l' in 'lie'">l</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span></span>/</a></span></span>) (January&#160;30, 1882&#160;– April&#160;12, 1945), commonly known by his initials <b>FDR</b>, was an American statesman and political leader who served as the <a href="/wiki/List_of_Presidents_of_the_United_States" title="List of Presidents of the United States">32nd President of the United States</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-PBS-20140910_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PBS-20140910-2"><span>[</span>2<span>]</span></a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Party_(United_States)" title="Democratic Party (United States)">Democrat</a>, he won a record four elections and served from March&#160;1933 to his death in April&#160;1945. He was a central figure in world events during the mid-20th century, leading the United States during a time of worldwide economic depression and total war. His program for relief, recovery and reform, known as the <a href="/wiki/New_Deal" title="New Deal">New Deal</a>, involved the great expansion of the role of the federal government in the economy. A dominant leader of the Democratic Party, he built the <a href="/wiki/New_Deal_Coalition" title="New Deal Coalition" class="mw-redirect">New Deal Coalition</a> that united labor unions, big city machines, white ethnics, African Americans, and rural white Southerners. The Coalition realigned American politics after 1932, creating the <a href="/wiki/Fifth_Party_System" title="Fifth Party System">Fifth Party System</a> and defining <a href="/wiki/Modern_liberalism_in_the_United_States" title="Modern liberalism in the United States">American liberalism</a> for the middle third of the 20th century.</p>
<p>Roosevelt was born in 1882 to <a href="/wiki/Roosevelt_family" title="Roosevelt family">an old, prominent Dutch family from upstate New York</a>. He attended the elite institutions of <a href="/wiki/Groton_School" title="Groton School">Groton School</a> and <a href="/wiki/Harvard_College" title="Harvard College">Harvard College</a>. In 1905, he married <a href="/wiki/Eleanor_Roosevelt" title="Eleanor Roosevelt">Eleanor Roosevelt</a>, with whom he had six children. He entered politics in 1910, serving in the <a href="/wiki/New_York_State_Senate" title="New York State Senate">New York State Senate</a>, and then as <a href="/wiki/Assistant_Secretary_of_the_Navy" title="Assistant Secretary of the Navy">Assistant Secretary of the Navy</a> under President <a href="/wiki/Woodrow_Wilson" title="Woodrow Wilson">Woodrow Wilson</a>. In 1920, Roosevelt ran for vice president alongside presidential candidate <a href="/wiki/James_M._Cox" title="James M. Cox">James M. Cox</a> but the <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1920" title="United States presidential election, 1920">Cox/Roosevelt ticket lost</a> to the Republican ticket of <a href="/wiki/Warren_Harding" title="Warren Harding" class="mw-redirect">Warren Harding</a> and <a href="/wiki/Calvin_Coolidge" title="Calvin Coolidge">Calvin Coolidge</a>. Roosevelt was <a href="/wiki/Franklin_D._Roosevelt%27s_paralytic_illness" title="Franklin D. Roosevelt's paralytic illness">stricken with polio</a> in 1921, which cost him the use of his legs and put his political career on hold for several years. Roosevelt attempted to recover from this illness, and founded a <a href="/wiki/Warm_Springs_Historic_District" title="Warm Springs Historic District">treatment center</a> for people with polio in <a href="/wiki/Warm_Springs,_Georgia" title="Warm Springs, Georgia">Warm Springs, Georgia</a>. After returning to political life by placing <a href="/wiki/Alfred_E._Smith" title="Alfred E. Smith" class="mw-redirect">Alfred E. Smith</a>'s name into nomination at the <a href="/wiki/1924_Democratic_National_Convention" title="1924 Democratic National Convention">1924 Democratic National Convention</a>, Roosevelt was asked by Smith to run for Governor of New York in the 1928 election. Roosevelt served as a reform governor from 1929 to 1932, and promoted the enactment of programs to combat the <a href="/wiki/Great_Depression_in_the_United_States" title="Great Depression in the United States">Great Depression</a> that occurred during his governorship.</p>
<p>Roosevelt defeated incumbent Republican president <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Hoover" title="Herbert Hoover">Herbert Hoover</a> in <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1932" title="United States presidential election, 1932">November&#160;1932</a>, at the depth of the Great Depression. Energized by his personal victory over <a href="/wiki/Polio" title="Polio" class="mw-redirect">polio</a>, FDR used his persistent optimism and activism to renew the national spirit.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3"><span>[</span>3<span>]</span></a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/First_100_days_of_Franklin_D._Roosevelt%27s_presidency" title="First 100 days of Franklin D. Roosevelt's presidency">his first hundred days</a> in office, which began March&#160;4, 1933, Roosevelt spearheaded major legislation and issued a profusion of executive orders that instituted the New Deal—a variety of programs designed to produce relief (government jobs for the unemployed), recovery (economic growth), and reform (through regulation of Wall Street, banks and transportation). He created numerous programs to support the unemployed and farmers, and to encourage labor union growth while more closely regulating business and high finance. The repeal of Prohibition added to his popularity, helping him win reelection by a landslide in 1936. The economy improved rapidly from 1933 to 1937, but then relapsed into a deep recession in 1937–38. The bipartisan <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Coalition" title="Conservative Coalition" class="mw-redirect">Conservative Coalition</a> that formed in 1937 prevented his <a href="/wiki/Judiciary_Reorganization_Bill_of_1937" title="Judiciary Reorganization Bill of 1937" class="mw-redirect">packing the Supreme Court</a>, blocked all proposals for major liberal legislation (apart from a minimum wage law), and abolished many of the relief programs when unemployment practically vanished during World War II. Most of the regulations on business continued in effect until they ended about 1975–85, except for the regulation of <a href="/wiki/Wall_Street" title="Wall Street">Wall Street</a> by the still existing <a href="/wiki/Securities_and_Exchange_Commission" title="Securities and Exchange Commission" class="mw-redirect">Securities and Exchange Commission</a>. Along with several smaller programs, major surviving programs include the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Deposit_Insurance_Corporation" title="Federal Deposit Insurance Corporation">Federal Deposit Insurance Corporation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Social_Security_(United_States)" title="Social Security (United States)">Social Security</a>.</p>
<p>As World War II loomed after 1938, with the Japanese invasion of China and the aggression of Nazi Germany, Roosevelt gave strong diplomatic and financial support to China and the United Kingdom, while remaining officially neutral. His goal was to make America the "<a href="/wiki/Arsenal_of_Democracy" title="Arsenal of Democracy">Arsenal of Democracy</a>", which would supply munitions to the Allies. In March&#160;1941, Roosevelt, with Congressional approval, provided <a href="/wiki/Lend-Lease" title="Lend-Lease">Lend-Lease</a> aid to Britain and China. After the Japanese <a href="/wiki/Attack_on_Pearl_Harbor" title="Attack on Pearl Harbor">attack on Pearl Harbor</a> on December&#160;7, 1941, which he called a "<a href="/wiki/Infamy_Speech" title="Infamy Speech">date which will live in infamy</a>", he made war on Japan and Germany. Assisted by his top aide <a href="/wiki/Harry_Hopkins" title="Harry Hopkins">Harry Hopkins</a>, and with very strong national support, he worked closely with British Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Winston_Churchill" title="Winston Churchill">Winston Churchill</a> and Soviet leader <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Stalin" title="Joseph Stalin">Joseph Stalin</a> in leading the <a href="/wiki/Allies_of_World_War_II" title="Allies of World War II">Allies</a> against <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Germany" title="Nazi Germany">Nazi Germany</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Italy" title="Kingdom of Italy">Fascist Italy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Empire_of_Japan" title="Empire of Japan">Imperial Japan</a> in <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>. He supervised the mobilization of the U.S. economy to support the war effort, and also ordered the <a href="/wiki/Japanese_American_internment" title="Japanese American internment" class="mw-redirect">internment</a> of 100,000 <a href="/wiki/Japanese_American" title="Japanese American" class="mw-redirect">Japanese American</a> civilians. As an active military leader, Roosevelt implemented a war strategy on two fronts that ended in the defeat of the <a href="/wiki/Axis_Powers" title="Axis Powers" class="mw-redirect">Axis Powers</a> and the development of the world's first <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_bomb" title="Nuclear bomb" class="mw-redirect">nuclear bomb</a>. His work also influenced the later creation of the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bretton_Woods_system" title="Bretton Woods system">Bretton Woods</a>. During the war, unemployment dropped to 2%, relief programs largely ended, and the industrial economy grew rapidly to new heights as millions of people moved to wartime factory jobs or entered military service.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4"><span>[</span>4<span>]</span></a></sup> Roosevelt's health seriously declined during the war years, and he died three months into his fourth term. He is often rated by scholars as one of the <a href="/wiki/Historical_rankings_of_Presidents_of_the_United_States" title="Historical rankings of Presidents of the United States">top three U.S. Presidents</a>, along with Abraham Lincoln and George Washington.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-54"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">14</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a>
<ul>
<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-55"><a href="#Official"><span class="tocnumber">14.1</span> <span class="toctext">Official</span></a></li>
<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-56"><a href="#Interviews.2C_speeches_and_statements"><span class="tocnumber">14.2</span> <span class="toctext">Interviews, speeches and statements</span></a></li>
<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-57"><a href="#Media_coverage"><span class="tocnumber">14.3</span> <span class="toctext">Media coverage</span></a></li>

</ul>
</li>
</ul>
</div>
<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Personal_life">Personal life</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Roosevelt_family" title="Roosevelt family">Roosevelt family</a> and <a href="/wiki/Delano_family" title="Delano family">Delano family</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life_and_education">Early life and education</span></h3>
<p>One of the oldest <a href="/wiki/Dutch_American" title="Dutch American">Dutch</a> families in New York State, the Roosevelts<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7"><span>[</span>a<span>]</span></a></sup> distinguished themselves in areas other than politics. One ancestor, Isaac Roosevelt, had served with the New York militia during the American Revolution.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurns19567_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurns19567-8"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup> Roosevelt attended events of the New York society <a href="/wiki/Sons_of_the_American_Revolution" title="Sons of the American Revolution">Sons of the American Revolution</a>, and joined the organization while he was president. His paternal family had become prosperous early on in New York real estate and trade, and much of his immediate family's wealth had been built by FDR's maternal grandfather, Warren Delano, Jr., in the China trade, including <a href="/wiki/Opium" title="Opium">opium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tea" title="Tea">tea</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlack2005.5Bhttps:.2F.2Fbooks.google.com.2Fbooks.3Fid.3DlYVCi70HaigC.26pg.3DPA4_4.E2.80.935.2C_9.E2.80.9310.5D_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlack2005.5Bhttps:.2F.2Fbooks.google.com.2Fbooks.3Fid.3DlYVCi70HaigC.26pg.3DPA4_4.E2.80.935.2C_9.E2.80.9310.5D-9"><span>[</span>8<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Franklin-Roosevelt-1884.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Franklin-Roosevelt-1884.jpg/170px-Franklin-Roosevelt-1884.jpg" width="170" height="218" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Franklin-Roosevelt-1884.jpg/255px-Franklin-Roosevelt-1884.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Franklin-Roosevelt-1884.jpg/340px-Franklin-Roosevelt-1884.jpg 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="768" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Franklin-Roosevelt-1884.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Roosevelt in 1884, 2 years old.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11"><span>[</span>b<span>]</span></a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Roosevelt was born on January&#160;30, 1882, in the <a href="/wiki/Hudson_Valley" title="Hudson Valley">Hudson Valley</a> town of <a href="/wiki/Hyde_Park,_New_York" title="Hyde Park, New York">Hyde Park, New York</a> to businessman <a href="/wiki/James_Roosevelt_I" title="James Roosevelt I">James Roosevelt I</a> (1828–1900) and <a href="/wiki/Sara_Roosevelt" title="Sara Roosevelt">Sara Ann Delano</a> (1854–1941). His parents were sixth cousins<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurns19567_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurns19567-8"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup> and both were from wealthy old New York families. They were of mostly English descent; Roosevelt's patrilineal great-grandfather, <a href="/wiki/James_Roosevelt_(1760%E2%80%931847)" title="James Roosevelt (1760–1847)">Jacobus Roosevelt III</a>, was of Dutch ancestry, and his mother's maiden name, <a href="/wiki/Delano_family" title="Delano family">Delano</a>, could be traced to a French <a href="/wiki/Huguenot" title="Huguenot">Huguenot</a> immigrant ancestor of the 17th century.<sup id="cite_ref-Roosevelt_Genealogy_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Roosevelt_Genealogy-12"><span>[</span>10<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13"><span>[</span>11<span>]</span></a></sup> Their only child<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14"><span>[</span>12<span>]</span></a></sup> was to have been named Warren, but Sara's infant nephew of that name had recently died.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGunther1950158.E2.80.9359_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGunther1950158.E2.80.9359-15"><span>[</span>13<span>]</span></a></sup> Their son was named for Sara's uncle Franklin Hughes Delano.<sup id="cite_ref-Roosevelt_Genealogy_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Roosevelt_Genealogy-12"><span>[</span>10<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Roosevelt grew up in an atmosphere of privilege (reportedly, when James Roosevelt took his five years old young son<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16"><span>[</span>14<span>]</span></a></sup> to visit President <a href="/wiki/Grover_Cleveland" title="Grover Cleveland">Grover Cleveland</a> in the White House, the busy president told Franklin, "I have one wish for you, little man, that you will never be President of the United States."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGunther1950168_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGunther1950168-17"><span>[</span>15<span>]</span></a></sup>) Sara was a possessive mother; James, 54 when Franklin was born, was considered by some as a remote father, though biographer <a href="/wiki/James_MacGregor_Burns" title="James MacGregor Burns">James MacGregor Burns</a> indicates James interacted with his son more than was typical at the time.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurns19564_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurns19564-18"><span>[</span>16<span>]</span></a></sup> Sara was the dominant influence in Franklin's early years;<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELash1971111_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELash1971111-19"><span>[</span>17<span>]</span></a></sup> she once declared, "My son Franklin is a Delano, not a Roosevelt at all."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurns19567_8-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurns19567-8"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup> Frequent trips to Europe—he made his first at the age of two, and went with his parents every year from the ages of seven to 15<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGunther1950169_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGunther1950169-20"><span>[</span>18<span>]</span></a></sup>—<a href="/wiki/List_of_Presidents_of_the_United_States_by_languages_spoken" title="List of Presidents of the United States by languages spoken" class="mw-redirect">helped Roosevelt become conversant</a> in German and French;<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith200720_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith200720-21"><span>[</span>19<span>]</span></a></sup> being arrested with his tutor by police four times in one day in the <a href="/wiki/Black_Forest" title="Black Forest">Black Forest</a> for minor offenses may have affected the future president's view of German character.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGunther1950170_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGunther1950170-22"><span>[</span>20<span>]</span></a></sup> He learned to ride, shoot, row, and play polo and lawn tennis. Roosevelt also took up golf in his teen years, becoming a skilled long hitter.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith2007110_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith2007110-23"><span>[</span>21<span>]</span></a></sup> He learned to sail, and his father gave him a sailboat at the age of 16 which he named "New Moon".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlack200521_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlack200521-24"><span>[</span>22<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Roosevelt attended <a href="/wiki/Groton_School" title="Groton School">Groton School</a>, an Episcopal boarding school in Massachusetts; 90% of the students were from families on the <a href="/wiki/Social_register" title="Social register" class="mw-redirect">social register</a>. He was strongly influenced by its headmaster, <a href="/wiki/Endicott_Peabody_(educator)" title="Endicott Peabody (educator)">Endicott Peabody</a>, who preached the duty of Christians to help the less fortunate and urged his students to enter public service. Forty years later Roosevelt said of Peabody, "It was a blessing in my life to have the privilege of [his] guiding hand",<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurns195616_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurns195616-25"><span>[</span>23<span>]</span></a></sup> and the headmaster remained a strong influence throughout his life, officiating at his wedding and visiting Roosevelt as president.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGunther1950174_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGunther1950174-26"><span>[</span>24<span>]</span></a></sup> Peabody recalled Roosevelt as "a quiet, satisfactory boy of more than ordinary intelligence, taking a good position in his form but not brilliant",<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith200728_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith200728-27"><span>[</span>25<span>]</span></a></sup> while a classmate described Roosevelt as "nice, but completely colorless"; an average student, he only stood out in being the only Democratic student, continuing the political tradition of his side of the Roosevelt family.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGunther1950172.E2.80.9373.2C_202_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGunther1950172.E2.80.9373.2C_202-28"><span>[</span>26<span>]</span></a></sup> Roosevelt remained consistent in his politics; immediately after his fourth election to the presidency, he defined his domestic policy as "a little left of center".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGunther1950351_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGunther1950351-29"><span>[</span>27<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30"><span>[</span>28<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Like all but two of his 21 Groton classmates, Roosevelt went to <a href="/wiki/Harvard_College" title="Harvard College">Harvard College</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGunther1950172_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGunther1950172-31"><span>[</span>29<span>]</span></a></sup> where he lived in a suite which is now part of <a href="/wiki/FDR_Suite_at_Adams_House,_Harvard_University" title="FDR Suite at Adams House, Harvard University">Adams House</a>, in the "Gold Coast" area populated by wealthy students. Again an average student academically,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith200730_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith200730-32"><span>[</span>30<span>]</span></a></sup> Roosevelt later declared, "I took economics courses in college for four years, and everything I was taught was wrong."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurns195618.2C_20_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurns195618.2C_20-33"><span>[</span>31<span>]</span></a></sup> He was a member of the <a href="/wiki/Alpha_Delta_Phi" title="Alpha Delta Phi">Alpha Delta Phi</a> fraternity<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34"><span>[</span>32<span>]</span></a></sup> and the Fly Club.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGunther1950176_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGunther1950176-35"><span>[</span>33<span>]</span></a></sup> While undistinguished as a student or athlete, he became editor-in-chief of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Harvard_Crimson" title="The Harvard Crimson">The Harvard Crimson</a></i> daily newspaper,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith200731.E2.80.9332_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith200731.E2.80.9332-36"><span>[</span>34<span>]</span></a></sup> a position which required great ambition, energy, and ability to manage others.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGunther1950175_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGunther1950175-37"><span>[</span>35<span>]</span></a></sup> While he was at Harvard, his fifth cousin <a href="/wiki/Theodore_Roosevelt" title="Theodore Roosevelt">Theodore "T.&#160;R." Roosevelt, Jr.</a> (1858–1919) became President of the United States; his vigorous leadership style and reforming zeal made him Franklin's role model and hero.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38"><span>[</span>36<span>]</span></a></sup> The younger Roosevelt remained a Democrat, campaigning for Theodore's opponent <a href="/wiki/William_Jennings_Bryan" title="William Jennings Bryan">William Jennings Bryan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGunther1950177_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGunther1950177-39"><span>[</span>37<span>]</span></a></sup> In mid-1902, Franklin was formally introduced to his future wife <a href="/wiki/Eleanor_Roosevelt" title="Eleanor Roosevelt">Anna Eleanor Roosevelt</a> (1884–1962), Theodore's niece, on a train to <a href="/wiki/Tivoli,_New_York" title="Tivoli, New York">Tivoli, New York</a>, although they had met briefly as children.<sup id="cite_ref-1884ER_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1884ER-40"><span>[</span>38<span>]</span></a></sup> Eleanor and Franklin were fifth cousins, once removed.<sup id="cite_ref-5thcousins_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5thcousins-41"><span>[</span>39<span>]</span></a></sup> She was the daughter of <a href="/wiki/Elliott_B._Roosevelt" title="Elliott B. Roosevelt" class="mw-redirect">Elliott Bulloch Roosevelt</a> (1860–94) and <a href="/wiki/Anna_Hall_Roosevelt" title="Anna Hall Roosevelt">Anna Rebecca Hall</a> (1863–92) of the <a href="/wiki/Livingston_family" title="Livingston family">Livingston family</a>. At the time of their engagement, Roosevelt was twenty-two and Eleanor nineteen.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurns195626_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurns195626-42"><span>[</span>40<span>]</span></a></sup> Roosevelt graduated from Harvard in 1903 with an <a href="/wiki/Bachelor_of_Arts" title="Bachelor of Arts">A.B.</a> in history. He later received an honorary <a href="/wiki/Legum_Doctor" title="Legum Doctor">LL.D.</a> from Harvard in 1929.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43"><span>[</span>41<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Roosevelt entered <a href="/wiki/Columbia_Law_School" title="Columbia Law School">Columbia Law School</a> in 1904, dropping out in 1907 after passing the New York State Bar exam.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurns_195628_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurns_195628-44"><span>[</span>42<span>]</span></a></sup> He later received a posthumous <a href="/wiki/Juris_Doctor" title="Juris Doctor">J.D.</a> from Columbia Law School.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45"><span>[</span>43<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1908, he took a job with the prestigious Wall Street firm of <a href="/wiki/Carter_Ledyard_%26_Milburn" title="Carter Ledyard &amp; Milburn">Carter Ledyard &amp; Milburn</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurns195628_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurns195628-46"><span>[</span>44<span>]</span></a></sup> dealing mainly with corporate law. He was first initiated in the <a href="/wiki/Independent_Order_of_Odd_Fellows" title="Independent Order of Odd Fellows">Independent Order of Odd Fellows</a> and was initiated into <a href="/wiki/Freemasonry" title="Freemasonry">Freemasonry</a> on October&#160;11, 1911, at Holland Lodge No. 8, New York City.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47"><span>[</span>45<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48"><span>[</span>46<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Marriage_and_affairs">Marriage and affairs</span></h3>
<p>On March&#160;17, 1905, Roosevelt married Eleanor despite the fierce resistance of his mother.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurns195626_42-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurns195626-42"><span>[</span>40<span>]</span></a></sup> While she did not dislike Eleanor, Sara Roosevelt was very possessive of her son; believing he was too young, she several times attempted to break the engagement.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGunther1950181.E2.80.9383_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGunther1950181.E2.80.9383-49"><span>[</span>47<span>]</span></a></sup> Eleanor's uncle, the president, stood in at the wedding for Eleanor's deceased father <a href="/wiki/Elliott_B._Roosevelt" title="Elliott B. Roosevelt" class="mw-redirect">Elliott</a>, as Eleanor was his favorite niece.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50"><span>[</span>48<span>]</span></a></sup> (Eleanor had lost both parents by age ten.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurns195627_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurns195627-51"><span>[</span>49<span>]</span></a></sup>)</p>
<p>The young couple moved into <a href="/wiki/Home_of_Franklin_D._Roosevelt_National_Historic_Site" title="Home of Franklin D. Roosevelt National Historic Site">Springwood</a>, his family's estate, where Roosevelt's mother became a frequent house guest, much to Eleanor's chagrin. The home was owned by Roosevelt's mother until her death in 1941 and was very much her home as well. In addition, Franklin Roosevelt and his mother Sara did the planning and furnishing of a town house she had built for the young couple in New York City; she had a twin house built alongside, with connections on every floor. Eleanor never felt it was her house.</p>
<p>Biographer James MacGregor Burns says young Roosevelt was self-assured and at ease in the upper class.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurns195677.E2.80.9379_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurns195677.E2.80.9379-52"><span>[</span>50<span>]</span></a></sup> In contrast, Eleanor at the time was shy and disliked social life, and at first stayed at home to raise their several children. Although Eleanor had an aversion to sexual intercourse, and considered it "an ordeal to be endured",<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWinkler200619.E2.80.9320_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWinkler200619.E2.80.9320-53"><span>[</span>51<span>]</span></a></sup> they had six children, the first four in rapid succession:</p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Anna_Roosevelt_Halsted" title="Anna Roosevelt Halsted">Anna Eleanor Roosevelt</a> (May&#160;3, 1906&#160;– December&#160;1, 1975)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/James_Roosevelt" title="James Roosevelt">James Roosevelt</a> II (December&#160;23, 1907&#160;– August&#160;13, 1991)</li>
<li>Franklin Roosevelt (March&#160;18, 1909&#160;– November&#160;7, 1909)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Elliott_Roosevelt" title="Elliott Roosevelt">Elliott Roosevelt</a> (September&#160;23, 1910&#160;– October&#160;27, 1990)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Franklin_Delano_Roosevelt,_Jr." title="Franklin Delano Roosevelt, Jr.">Franklin Delano Roosevelt, Jr.</a> (August&#160;17, 1914&#160;– August&#160;17, 1988)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/John_Aspinwall_Roosevelt" title="John Aspinwall Roosevelt">John Aspinwall Roosevelt</a> II (March&#160;13, 1916&#160;– April&#160;27, 1981)</li>
</ul>
<p>Roosevelt liked fatherhood, and the parents suffered greatly when their third child, named for Franklin, died of heart disease in infancy in 1909. Eleanor soon was pregnant again and gave birth to another son, Elliott, less than a year later. The fifth child and fourth son, born in 1914, was also named for Franklin. Their sixth child, John, was their fifth son.</p>
<p>Roosevelt had affairs outside his marriage, including one with Eleanor's social secretary <a href="/wiki/Lucy_Mercer_Rutherfurd" title="Lucy Mercer Rutherfurd">Lucy Mercer</a>, which began soon after she was hired in early 1914.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith2007153_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith2007153-54"><span>[</span>52<span>]</span></a></sup> In September&#160;1918, Eleanor found letters revealing the affair in Roosevelt's luggage, when he returned from <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>. Franklin had contemplated divorcing Eleanor, but Lucy would not agree to marry a divorced man with five children.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith2007160_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith2007160-55"><span>[</span>53<span>]</span></a></sup> Franklin and Eleanor remained married, and FDR promised never to see Lucy again. Eleanor never truly forgave him, and their marriage from that point on was more of a political partnership. His mother Sara told Franklin that if he divorced his wife, it would bring scandal upon the family, and she "would not give him another dollar."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith2007160_55-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith2007160-55"><span>[</span>53<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Franklin broke his promise to Eleanor. He and Lucy maintained a formal correspondence, and began seeing each other again in 1941, perhaps earlier.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56"><span>[</span>54<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57"><span>[</span>55<span>]</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secret_Service" title="United States Secret Service">Secret Service</a> gave Lucy the code name "Mrs. Johnson" .<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith2007163_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith2007163-58"><span>[</span>56<span>]</span></a></sup> Lucy was with FDR on the day he died. Despite this, FDR's affair was not widely known until the 1960s.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWinkler2006202.E2.80.933_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWinkler2006202.E2.80.933-59"><span>[</span>57<span>]</span></a></sup> Roosevelt's son Elliott said that Franklin also had a 20-year affair with his private secretary <a href="/wiki/Marguerite_LeHand" title="Marguerite LeHand">Marguerite "Missy" LeHand</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60"><span>[</span>58<span>]</span></a></sup> Another son, James, stated that "there is a real possibility that a romantic relationship existed" between his father and <a href="/wiki/Princess_M%C3%A4rtha_of_Sweden" title="Princess Märtha of Sweden">Princess Märtha of Sweden</a>, who resided in the White House during part of World War II. Aides began to refer to her at the time as "the president's girlfriend",<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoodwin1995153_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoodwin1995153-61"><span>[</span>59<span>]</span></a></sup> and gossip linking the two romantically appeared in the newspapers.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERowley2010254_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERowley2010254-62"><span>[</span>60<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The effect of these flirtations or affairs upon Eleanor Roosevelt is difficult to estimate. "I have the memory of an elephant. I can forgive, but I cannot forget," she wrote to a close friend.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWinkler200638_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWinkler200638-63"><span>[</span>61<span>]</span></a></sup> After the Lucy Mercer affair, any remaining intimacy left their relationship. Eleanor soon thereafter established a separate house in Hyde Park at <a href="/wiki/Eleanor_Roosevelt_National_Historic_Site" title="Eleanor Roosevelt National Historic Site">Val-Kill</a>, and increasingly devoted herself to various social and political causes. For the rest of their lives, the Roosevelts' marriage was more of a political partnership than an intimate relationship.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWinkler200628.2C_38.2C_48.E2.80.9349_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWinkler200628.2C_38.2C_48.E2.80.9349-64"><span>[</span>62<span>]</span></a></sup> The emotional break in their marriage was so severe that when Roosevelt asked Eleanor in 1942—in light of his failing health—to come back home and live with him again, she refused.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWinkler2006202.E2.80.933_59-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWinkler2006202.E2.80.933-59"><span>[</span>57<span>]</span></a></sup> He was not always aware of when she visited the White House, and for some time she could not easily reach him on the telephone without his secretary's help; he, in turn, did not visit her New York City apartment until late 1944.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGunther1950195_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGunther1950195-65"><span>[</span>63<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>When Roosevelt was President, his dog, <a href="/wiki/Fala_(dog)" title="Fala (dog)">Fala</a>, also became well known as his companion during his time in the White House. Fala was called the "most photographed dog in the world."<sup id="cite_ref-wash_post_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wash_post-66"><span>[</span>64<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 242.66666666667px">
<div style="width: 242.66666666667px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 240.66666666667px;">

</div>
