<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_political_career">Early political career</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="State_senator_and_Tammany_antagonist">State senator and Tammany antagonist</span></h3>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/New_York_state_election,_1910" title="New York state election, 1910">state election of 1910</a>, Roosevelt ran for the <a href="/wiki/New_York_State_Senate" title="New York State Senate">New York State Senate</a> from the district around <a href="/wiki/Hyde_Park,_New_York" title="Hyde Park, New York">Hyde Park</a> in <a href="/wiki/Dutchess_County,_New_York" title="Dutchess County, New York">Dutchess County</a>, which was strongly Republican, having elected one Democrat since 1856. The local party chose him as a <a href="/wiki/Paper_candidate" title="Paper candidate">paper candidate</a> because his Republican cousin Theodore was still one of the country's most prominent politicians, and a Democratic Roosevelt was good publicity; the candidate could also pay for his own campaign. Surprising almost everyone, due to his aggressive and effective campaign,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGunther1950202.E2.80.933_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGunther1950202.E2.80.933-67"><span>[</span>65<span>]</span></a></sup> the Roosevelt name's influence in the Hudson Valley, and the Democratic landslide that year, Roosevelt won the election.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurns195634_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurns195634-68"><span>[</span>66<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Taking his seat on January&#160;1, 1911, Roosevelt immediately became the leader of a group of "Insurgents" who opposed the <a href="/wiki/Bossism" title="Bossism" class="mw-redirect">bossism</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Tammany_Hall" title="Tammany Hall">Tammany</a> <a href="/wiki/Political_machine" title="Political machine">machine</a> dominating the state Democratic Party. The <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate_election_in_New_York,_1911" title="United States Senate election in New York, 1911">U.S. Senate election</a>, which began with the Democratic caucus on January&#160;16, 1911, was deadlocked by the struggle of the two factions for 74 days, as the new legislator endured what a biographer later described as "the full might of Tammany" behind its choice, <a href="/wiki/William_F._Sheehan" title="William F. Sheehan">William F. Sheehan</a>. (Popular election of US Senators did not occur until after a constitutional amendment.) On March&#160;31 compromise candidate <a href="/wiki/James_A._O%27Gorman" title="James A. O'Gorman" class="mw-redirect">James A. O'Gorman</a> was elected, giving Roosevelt national exposure and some experience in political tactics and intrigue; one Tammany leader warned that Roosevelt should be eliminated immediately, before he disrupted Democrats as much as his cousin <a href="/wiki/Bull_Moose_Party" title="Bull Moose Party" class="mw-redirect">disrupted the Republicans</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurns195640_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurns195640-69"><span>[</span>67<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGunther1950203.E2.80.935_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGunther1950203.E2.80.935-70"><span>[</span>68<span>]</span></a></sup> Roosevelt soon became a popular figure among New York Democrats, though he had not as yet become an eloquent speaker.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurns195634_68-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurns195634-68"><span>[</span>66<span>]</span></a></sup> News articles and cartoons began depicting "the second coming of a Roosevelt" that sent "cold shivers down the spine of Tammany".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGunther1950205.E2.80.936_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGunther1950205.E2.80.936-71"><span>[</span>69<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Despite a bout of <a href="/wiki/Typhoid_fever" title="Typhoid fever">typhoid fever</a>, and thanks to the help of <a href="/wiki/Louis_McHenry_Howe" title="Louis McHenry Howe" class="mw-redirect">Louis McHenry Howe</a> who ran his campaign, Roosevelt was re-elected for a second term in the <a href="/wiki/New_York_state_election,_1912" title="New York state election, 1912">state election of 1912</a>, and served as chairman of the Agriculture Committee. His success with farm and labor bills was a precursor to his New Deal policies twenty years later.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurns195644.E2.80.9346_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurns195644.E2.80.9346-72"><span>[</span>70<span>]</span></a></sup> By this time he had become more consistently progressive, in support of labor and social welfare programs for women and children; cousin Theodore was of some influence on these issues.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurns195643_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurns195643-73"><span>[</span>71<span>]</span></a></sup> Roosevelt, again in opposition to Tammany Hall, supported southerner <a href="/wiki/Woodrow_Wilson" title="Woodrow Wilson">Woodrow Wilson</a>'s successful bid in the 1912 presidential election, and thereby earned an informal designation as an original Wilson man.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurns195649_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurns195649-74"><span>[</span>72<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Assistant_Secretary_of_the_Navy">Assistant Secretary of the Navy</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Franklin_Roosevelt_Secretary_of_the_Navy_1913.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Franklin_Roosevelt_Secretary_of_the_Navy_1913.jpg/170px-Franklin_Roosevelt_Secretary_of_the_Navy_1913.jpg" width="170" height="247" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Franklin_Roosevelt_Secretary_of_the_Navy_1913.jpg/255px-Franklin_Roosevelt_Secretary_of_the_Navy_1913.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Franklin_Roosevelt_Secretary_of_the_Navy_1913.jpg/340px-Franklin_Roosevelt_Secretary_of_the_Navy_1913.jpg 2x" data-file-width="944" data-file-height="1370" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Franklin_Roosevelt_Secretary_of_the_Navy_1913.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Roosevelt as Assistant Secretary of the Navy, 1913.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Roosevelt's support of Wilson led to his appointment in 1913 as <a href="/wiki/Assistant_Secretary_of_the_Navy" title="Assistant Secretary of the Navy">Assistant Secretary of the Navy</a><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith200751.E2.80.9398_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith200751.E2.80.9398-75"><span>[</span>73<span>]</span></a></sup> under <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_the_Navy" title="United States Secretary of the Navy">Secretary of the Navy</a> <a href="/wiki/Josephus_Daniels" title="Josephus Daniels">Josephus Daniels</a>. Roosevelt had a lifelong affection for the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Navy" title="United States Navy">Navy</a>—he had already collected almost 10,000 naval books and claimed to have read all but one—and was more ardent than his boss Daniels in supporting a large and efficient naval force.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurns195651_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurns195651-76"><span>[</span>74<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGunther1950209_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGunther1950209-77"><span>[</span>75<span>]</span></a></sup> As assistant secretary, Roosevelt worked to expand the Navy and founded the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Navy_Reserve" title="United States Navy Reserve">United States Navy Reserve</a>. Against reactionary older officers such as Admiral <a href="/wiki/William_S._Benson" title="William S. Benson">William Benson</a>—who claimed he could not "conceive of any use the fleet will ever have for aviation"—Roosevelt personally ordered the preservation of the navy's <a href="/wiki/Bureau_of_Aeronautics#Origins:_1920s_and_1930s" title="Bureau of Aeronautics">Aviation Division</a> after the war,<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78"><span>[</span>76<span>]</span></a></sup> despite publicly opining that <a href="/wiki/Billy_Mitchell" title="Billy Mitchell">Billy Mitchell</a>'s warnings of bombs capable of sinking <a href="/wiki/Battleship" title="Battleship">battleships</a> were "pernicious".<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79"><span>[</span>77<span>]</span></a></sup> Roosevelt negotiated with Congressional leaders and other government departments to get budgets approved. He opposed the <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Winslow_Taylor" title="Frederick Winslow Taylor">Taylor</a> "stop-watch" system, which was hailed by shipbuilding managers but opposed by the unions. Not a single union strike occurred during his seven-plus years in the office,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurns195652_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurns195652-80"><span>[</span>78<span>]</span></a></sup> during which Roosevelt gained experience in labor issues, government management during wartime, naval issues, and logistics, all valuable areas for future office.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGunther1950212_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGunther1950212-81"><span>[</span>79<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Roosevelt was still relatively obscure, but his friends were already speaking of him as a future president; he reportedly began talking about being elected to the presidency as early as 1907.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGunther1950206.2C_213.2C_262_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGunther1950206.2C_213.2C_262-82"><span>[</span>80<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1914, Roosevelt made an ill-conceived decision to run for the U.S. Senate seat for New York. The decision was doomed for lack of Wilson administration backing. He was determined to take on Tammany again at a time when Wilson needed them to help marshal his legislation and secure his future re-election.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurns195656_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurns195656-83"><span>[</span>81<span>]</span></a></sup> He was soundly defeated in the Democratic <a href="/wiki/Primary_election" title="Primary election">primary election</a> for the United States Senate by Tammany Hall-backed <a href="/wiki/James_W._Gerard" title="James W. Gerard">James W. Gerard</a>, by a margin of 3-to-1.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurns195657_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurns195657-84"><span>[</span>82<span>]</span></a></sup> Roosevelt learned a valuable lesson, that federal patronage alone, without White House support, could not defeat a strong local organization.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurns195660_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurns195660-85"><span>[</span>83<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In March&#160;1917, after Germany initiated its <a href="/wiki/Submarine" title="Submarine">submarine</a> warfare campaign, Roosevelt asked Wilson for permission, which was denied, to fit the naval fleet out for war.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurns195662_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurns195662-86"><span>[</span>84<span>]</span></a></sup> He became an enthusiastic advocate of the <a href="/wiki/Submarine" title="Submarine">submarine</a> and of means to combat the German submarine menace to Allied shipping: he proposed building a <a href="/wiki/North_Sea_Mine_Barrage" title="North Sea Mine Barrage">mine barrier across the North Sea</a> from Norway to Scotland.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith2007143_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith2007143-87"><span>[</span>85<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1918, he visited Scotland, England, Wales, and France to inspect American naval facilities.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith2007158_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith2007158-88"><span>[</span>86<span>]</span></a></sup> Roosevelt wanted to provide arms to the merchant marine; knowing that a sale of arms was prohibited, he asked Wilson for approval to lease the arms to the mariners. Wilson ultimately approved this by executive order, and a precedent was set for Roosevelt to take similar action in 1940.</p>
<p>During these war years, Roosevelt worked to make peace with the Tammany Hall forces, and in 1918 the group supported others in an unsuccessful attempt to persuade him to run for governor of New York. He very much wanted to get into a military uniform, but the armistice took shape before this could materialize;<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurns195664_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurns195664-89"><span>[</span>87<span>]</span></a></sup> Wilson reportedly ordered Roosevelt to not resign.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGunther1950214.E2.80.9315_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGunther1950214.E2.80.9315-90"><span>[</span>88<span>]</span></a></sup> With the end of <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a> in November&#160;1918, Roosevelt was in charge of demobilization, although he opposed plans to completely dismantle the Navy.</p>
<p>In 1918, Roosevelt was sickened during the <a href="/wiki/1918_flu_pandemic" title="1918 flu pandemic">1918 flu pandemic</a>, and survived.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECollier1974_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECollier1974-91"><span>[</span>89<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1919, newspapers in <a href="/wiki/Newport,_Rhode_Island" title="Newport, Rhode Island">Newport, Rhode Island</a> criticized Roosevelt over his handling of what came to be known as the <a href="/wiki/Newport_sex_scandal" title="Newport sex scandal">Newport sex scandal</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECook1992267.E2.80.9371_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECook1992267.E2.80.9371-92"><span>[</span>90<span>]</span></a></sup> Much more threatening was the fact that Roosevelt and his wife, then living in <a href="/wiki/Washington,_D.C." title="Washington, D.C.">Washington, D.C.</a> across the street from <a href="/wiki/Attorney_General" title="Attorney General" class="mw-redirect">Attorney General</a> <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Mitchell_Palmer" title="Alexander Mitchell Palmer" class="mw-redirect">Alexander Mitchell Palmer</a>, narrowly missed becoming casualties of an <a href="/wiki/1919_United_States_anarchist_bombings" title="1919 United States anarchist bombings">anarchist's bomb</a> that exploded at Palmer's house, which they had walked past just minutes before. Their own residence was close enough that one of the bomber's body parts landed on their doorstep.<sup id="cite_ref-Brands_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brands-93"><span>[</span>91<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Campaign_for_Vice_President">Campaign for Vice President</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Franklin_D._Roosevelt_and_James_Cox_in_Dayton,_Ohio_-_NARA_-_197236.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Franklin_D._Roosevelt_and_James_Cox_in_Dayton%2C_Ohio_-_NARA_-_197236.jpg/220px-Franklin_D._Roosevelt_and_James_Cox_in_Dayton%2C_Ohio_-_NARA_-_197236.jpg" width="220" height="143" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Franklin_D._Roosevelt_and_James_Cox_in_Dayton%2C_Ohio_-_NARA_-_197236.jpg/330px-Franklin_D._Roosevelt_and_James_Cox_in_Dayton%2C_Ohio_-_NARA_-_197236.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Franklin_D._Roosevelt_and_James_Cox_in_Dayton%2C_Ohio_-_NARA_-_197236.jpg/440px-Franklin_D._Roosevelt_and_James_Cox_in_Dayton%2C_Ohio_-_NARA_-_197236.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2833" data-file-height="1845" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Franklin_D._Roosevelt_and_James_Cox_in_Dayton,_Ohio_-_NARA_-_197236.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Cox and Roosevelt in Ohio, 1920.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/1920_Democratic_National_Convention" title="1920 Democratic National Convention">1920 Democratic National Convention</a> chose Roosevelt by acclamation as the <a href="/wiki/Vice_President_of_the_United_States" title="Vice President of the United States">vice-presidential candidate</a><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurns195672_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurns195672-94"><span>[</span>92<span>]</span></a></sup> with its presidential candidate, Governor <a href="/wiki/James_M._Cox" title="James M. Cox">James M. Cox</a> of Ohio. Although his nomination surprised most people, Roosevelt was considered as bringing balance to the ticket as a moderate, a Wilsonian, and a prohibitionist with a famous name.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurns195673_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurns195673-95"><span>[</span>93<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGunther1950215.E2.80.9316_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGunther1950215.E2.80.9316-96"><span>[</span>94<span>]</span></a></sup> Roosevelt had just turned 38, four years younger than Theodore had been when he received the same nomination from his party. The Cox-Roosevelt ticket was defeated by <a href="/wiki/United_States_Republican_Party" title="United States Republican Party" class="mw-redirect">Republicans</a> <a href="/wiki/Warren_G._Harding" title="Warren G. Harding">Warren G. Harding</a> and <a href="/wiki/Calvin_Coolidge" title="Calvin Coolidge">Calvin Coolidge</a> in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1920" title="United States presidential election, 1920">presidential election</a> by a wide margin.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurns195674_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurns195674-97"><span>[</span>95<span>]</span></a></sup> Roosevelt returned to New York to practice law and joined the newly organized New York <a href="/wiki/Civitan_International" title="Civitan International">Civitan</a> Club.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98"><span>[</span>96<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Polio">Polio</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Franklin_D._Roosevelt%27s_paralytic_illness" title="Franklin D. Roosevelt's paralytic illness">Franklin D. Roosevelt's paralytic illness</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Roosevelt_in_a_wheelchair.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Roosevelt_in_a_wheelchair.jpg/220px-Roosevelt_in_a_wheelchair.jpg" width="220" height="269" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Roosevelt_in_a_wheelchair.jpg/330px-Roosevelt_in_a_wheelchair.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Roosevelt_in_a_wheelchair.jpg/440px-Roosevelt_in_a_wheelchair.jpg 2x" data-file-width="463" data-file-height="566" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Roosevelt_in_a_wheelchair.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Rare photograph of FDR in a wheelchair, with Ruthie Bie and <a href="/wiki/Fala_(dog)" title="Fala (dog)">Fala</a>, taken by his cousin <a href="/wiki/Margaret_Suckley" title="Margaret Suckley">Margaret Suckley</a> (February 1941)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In August&#160;1921, while the Roosevelts were vacationing at <a href="/wiki/Campobello_Island" title="Campobello Island">Campobello Island</a>, New Brunswick, Canada, Roosevelt <a href="/wiki/Franklin_D._Roosevelt%27s_paralytic_illness" title="Franklin D. Roosevelt's paralytic illness">fell ill</a> and was diagnosed with <a href="/wiki/Poliomyelitis" title="Poliomyelitis">polio</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99"><span>[</span>97<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-The_Roosevelts_2014_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Roosevelts_2014-100"><span>[</span>98<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:236</sup> It left him with permanent paralysis from the waist down. Following the illness, Roosevelt remained out of the public eye for several years, turning his attention away from politics and toward his legal practice. For the rest of his life, Roosevelt refused to accept that he was permanently paralyzed.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurns195687_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurns195687-101"><span>[</span>99<span>]</span></a></sup> He tried a wide range of therapies, including <a href="/wiki/Hydrotherapy" title="Hydrotherapy">hydrotherapy</a>. In 1926, he purchased a resort at <a href="/wiki/Warm_Springs,_Georgia" title="Warm Springs, Georgia">Warm Springs, Georgia</a>, where he founded a hydrotherapy center for the treatment of polio patients; it still operates as the <a href="/wiki/Roosevelt_Warm_Springs_Institute_for_Rehabilitation" title="Roosevelt Warm Springs Institute for Rehabilitation" class="mw-redirect">Roosevelt Warm Springs Institute for Rehabilitation</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurns195688_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurns195688-102"><span>[</span>100<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1938 FDR founded the National Foundation for Infantile Paralysis, now known as the <a href="/wiki/March_of_Dimes" title="March of Dimes">March of Dimes</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith2007217_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith2007217-103"><span>[</span>101<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Baghdady_Spring_2008_60.E2.80.9365_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Baghdady_Spring_2008_60.E2.80.9365-104"><span>[</span>102<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>At the time, Roosevelt convinced many people that he was improving, which he believed to be essential prior to running for public office again.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERowley2010125_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERowley2010125-105"><span>[</span>103<span>]</span></a></sup> He laboriously taught himself to walk short distances while wearing iron braces on his hips and legs by swiveling his torso, supporting himself with a cane.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERowley2010120_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERowley2010120-106"><span>[</span>104<span>]</span></a></sup> He was careful never to be seen using his wheelchair in public, and great care was taken to prevent any portrayal in the press that would highlight his disability.<sup id="cite_ref-The_Roosevelts_2014_100-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Roosevelts_2014-100"><span>[</span>98<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:332</sup> Few photographs of FDR in his wheelchair are known; they include two taken by his cousin and confidante <a href="/wiki/Margaret_Suckley" title="Margaret Suckley">Margaret Suckley</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107"><span>[</span>105<span>]</span></a></sup> another taken by a sailor aboard the USS Indianapolis in 1933,<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108"><span>[</span>106<span>]</span></a></sup> and another published in a 1937 issue of <i><a href="/wiki/Life_(magazine)" title="Life (magazine)">Life</a></i> magazine.<sup id="cite_ref-The_Roosevelts_2014_100-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Roosevelts_2014-100"><span>[</span>98<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:332–333</sup> Film clips of the "walk" he achieved after his illness are equally rare.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109"><span>[</span>107<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110"><span>[</span>108<span>]</span></a></sup> He usually appeared in public standing upright, supported on one side by an aide or one of his sons. FDR used a car with specially designed hand controls, providing him further mobility.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111"><span>[</span>109<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>A 2003 <a href="/wiki/Retrospective_diagnosis" title="Retrospective diagnosis">retrospective diagnosis</a> of Roosevelt's paralytic illness favored <a href="/wiki/Guillain%E2%80%93Barr%C3%A9_syndrome" title="Guillain–Barré syndrome">Guillain–Barré syndrome</a> rather than polio,<sup id="cite_ref-Abstract_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Abstract-112"><span>[</span>110<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Goldman_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Goldman-113"><span>[</span>111<span>]</span></a></sup> a conclusion criticized by other researchers.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Governor_of_New_York_.281929.E2.80.9332.29">Governor of New York (1929–32)</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Governorship_of_Franklin_D._Roosevelt" title="Governorship of Franklin D. Roosevelt">Governorship of Franklin D. Roosevelt</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Governor_Roosevelt_and_Al_Smith.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Governor_Roosevelt_and_Al_Smith.jpg/170px-Governor_Roosevelt_and_Al_Smith.jpg" width="170" height="248" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Governor_Roosevelt_and_Al_Smith.jpg/255px-Governor_Roosevelt_and_Al_Smith.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Governor_Roosevelt_and_Al_Smith.jpg/340px-Governor_Roosevelt_and_Al_Smith.jpg 2x" data-file-width="446" data-file-height="650" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Governor_Roosevelt_and_Al_Smith.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Roosevelt with Al Smith in 1930.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Roosevelt maintained contacts and mended fences with the Democratic Party during the 1920s, especially in New York. Although he initially had made his name as an opponent of <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Tammany_Hall" title="Tammany Hall">Tammany Hall</a> <a href="/wiki/Political_machine" title="Political machine">machine</a>, Roosevelt moderated his stance against that group as well.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurns195693_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurns195693-114"><span>[</span>112<span>]</span></a></sup> He helped <a href="/wiki/Al_Smith" title="Al Smith">Alfred E. Smith</a> win the election for governor of New York in 1922, and in 1924 was a strong supporter of Smith against his cousin, Republican <a href="/wiki/Theodore_Roosevelt,_Jr." title="Theodore Roosevelt, Jr.">Theodore Roosevelt, Jr.</a><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurns195691.E2.80.9396_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurns195691.E2.80.9396-115"><span>[</span>113<span>]</span></a></sup> Roosevelt gave nominating speeches for Smith at the 1924 and 1928 Democratic conventions; the speech at the 1924 election marked a return to public life following his illness and convalescence.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorgan1985pp._267.2C_269.E2.80.9372.2C_286.E2.80.9387_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorgan1985pp._267.2C_269.E2.80.9372.2C_286.E2.80.9387-116"><span>[</span>114<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>As the Democratic Party presidential nominee in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1928" title="United States presidential election, 1928">1928 election</a>, Smith in turn asked Roosevelt to run for governor in the <a href="/wiki/New_York_state_election,_1928" title="New York state election, 1928">state election</a>. Roosevelt was nominated by the Democrats by acclamation.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurns1956100_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurns1956100-117"><span>[</span>115<span>]</span></a></sup> While Smith lost the Presidency in a landslide, and was defeated in his home state, Roosevelt was narrowly elected governor, by a one-percent margin.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurns1956101_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurns1956101-118"><span>[</span>116<span>]</span></a></sup> As a reform governor, he established a number of new social programs, and was advised by <a href="/wiki/Frances_Perkins" title="Frances Perkins">Frances Perkins</a> and <a href="/wiki/Harry_Hopkins" title="Harry Hopkins">Harry Hopkins</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith2007238.E2.80.9342_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith2007238.E2.80.9342-119"><span>[</span>117<span>]</span></a></sup> In April&#160;1929 a bomb was found addressed to him at the Albany, New York post office. A porter kicked the package, causing the fuse to sputter. The device was dropped in a pail of water where it failed to go off.<sup id="cite_ref-assassinationattempts_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-assassinationattempts-120"><span>[</span>118<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In May&#160;1930, as he began his run for a second term, Roosevelt reiterated his doctrine from the campaign two years before: "that progressive government by its very terms, must be a living and growing thing, that the battle for it is never ending and that if we let up for one single moment or one single year, not merely do we stand still but we fall back in the march of civilization."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurns_1956119.E2.80.9320_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurns_1956119.E2.80.9320-121"><span>[</span>119<span>]</span></a></sup> In this <a href="/wiki/New_York_state_election,_1930" title="New York state election, 1930">campaign</a> for re-election, Roosevelt needed the good will of the Tammany Hall machine in New York City to succeed; but, his Republican opponent, <a href="/wiki/Charles_H._Tuttle" title="Charles H. Tuttle">Charles H. Tuttle</a>, used Roosevelt's connection with Tammany Hall's corruption as an election issue. As the election approached, Roosevelt began preemptive efforts by initiating investigations of the sale of judicial offices. He was directly involved, as he had made a routine short-term court appointment of a Tammany Hall man who was alleged to have paid Tammany $30,000 for the position.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurns_1956119.E2.80.9320_121-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurns_1956119.E2.80.9320-121"><span>[</span>119<span>]</span></a></sup> His Republican opponent could not overcome the public's criticism of the Republican Party for current economic distress in the <a href="/wiki/Great_Depression" title="Great Depression">Great Depression</a>, and Roosevelt was elected to a second term by a margin of fourteen percent.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurns_1956121_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurns_1956121-122"><span>[</span>120<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="1932_presidential_election">1932 presidential election</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1932" title="United States presidential election, 1932">United States presidential election, 1932</a></div>
<p>Roosevelt's strong base in the most populous state made him an obvious candidate for the Democratic nomination, which was hotly contested in light of incumbent <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Hoover" title="Herbert Hoover">Herbert Hoover</a>'s vulnerability. Al Smith was supported by some city bosses, but had lost control of the New York Democratic party to Roosevelt. Roosevelt built his own national coalition with personal allies such as newspaper magnate <a href="/wiki/William_Randolph_Hearst" title="William Randolph Hearst">William Randolph Hearst</a>, Irish leader <a href="/wiki/Joseph_P._Kennedy,_Sr." title="Joseph P. Kennedy, Sr.">Joseph P. Kennedy, Sr.</a>, and California leader <a href="/wiki/William_Gibbs_McAdoo" title="William Gibbs McAdoo">William Gibbs McAdoo</a>. When Texas leader <a href="/wiki/John_Nance_Garner" title="John Nance Garner">John Nance Garner</a> announced his support of FDR, he was given the vice-presidential nomination.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith2007272.E2.80.9374_123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith2007272.E2.80.9374-123"><span>[</span>121<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Breaking with tradition of the time, Roosevelt traveled to Chicago to accept the nomination in person. In his acceptance speech, Roosevelt declared, "I pledge you, I pledge myself to a <a href="/wiki/New_Deal" title="New Deal">new deal</a> for the American people... This is more than a political campaign. It is a call to arms."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurns1956139_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurns1956139-124"><span>[</span>122<span>]</span></a></sup> The election campaign was conducted under the shadow of the <a href="/wiki/Great_Depression_in_the_United_States" title="Great Depression in the United States">Great Depression in the United States</a>, and the new alliances which it created. Roosevelt and the Democratic Party mobilized the expanded ranks of the poor as well as organized labor, ethnic minorities, urbanites, and Southern whites, crafting the <a href="/wiki/New_Deal_coalition" title="New Deal coalition">New Deal coalition</a>. At that time, African Americans in the South were still <a href="/wiki/Disfranchisement_after_Reconstruction_era" title="Disfranchisement after Reconstruction era" class="mw-redirect">disfranchised</a>, as they had been since the turn of the century. Southern states had passed a variety of requirements making voter registration more difficult, which served to exclude most blacks and many poor whites from the political system.</p>
<p>Economist <a href="/wiki/Marriner_Eccles" title="Marriner Eccles" class="mw-redirect">Marriner Eccles</a> observed that "given later developments, the campaign speeches often read like a giant misprint, in which Roosevelt and Hoover speak each other's lines."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKennedy1999102_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKennedy1999102-125"><span>[</span>123<span>]</span></a></sup> Roosevelt denounced Hoover's failures to restore prosperity or halt the downward slide, and he ridiculed Hoover's huge deficits. Roosevelt campaigned on the Democratic platform advocating "immediate and drastic reductions of all public expenditures," "abolishing useless commissions and offices, consolidating departments and bureaus, and eliminating extravagances" and for a "sound currency to be maintained at all hazards." On September&#160;23, Roosevelt made the gloomy evaluation that, "Our industrial plant is built; the problem just now is whether under existing conditions it is not overbuilt. Our last frontier has long since been reached."<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126"><span>[</span>124<span>]</span></a></sup> Hoover damned that pessimism as a denial of "the promise of American life... the counsel of despair."<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127"><span>[</span>125<span>]</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Prohibition_in_the_United_States" title="Prohibition in the United States">prohibition issue</a> solidified the "wet vote" for Roosevelt, who noted that <a href="/wiki/Repeal_of_Prohibition" title="Repeal of Prohibition" class="mw-redirect">repeal</a> would bring in new tax revenues.</p>
<p>Roosevelt won 57 percent of the vote and carried all but six states. Historians and political scientists consider the 1932–36 elections a <a href="/wiki/Realigning_election" title="Realigning election">realigning election</a> that created a new majority coalition for the Democrats, made up of organized labor, northern blacks, and ethnic Americans such as Italian-Americans, Polish-Americans and Jews. This transformed American politics and started what is called the "New Deal Party System" or (by political scientists) the <a href="/wiki/Fifth_Party_System" title="Fifth Party System">Fifth Party System</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128"><span>[</span>126<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>After the election, Roosevelt refused Hoover's requests for a meeting to develop a joint program to stop the downward spiral and calm investors, claiming publicly it would tie his hands, and that Hoover had all the power to act if necessary. Unofficially, he told reporters that "it is not my baby".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurns1956146_129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurns1956146-129"><span>[</span>127<span>]</span></a></sup> The economy spiraled downward until the banking system began a complete nationwide shutdown as Hoover's term ended.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurns1956148_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurns1956148-130"><span>[</span>128<span>]</span></a></sup> In February&#160;1933, Roosevelt escaped an assassination attempt. <a href="/wiki/Giuseppe_Zangara" title="Giuseppe Zangara">Giuseppe Zangara</a>, who expressed a "hate for all rulers," attempted to shoot Roosevelt. He shot and killed Chicago Mayor <a href="/wiki/Anton_Cermak" title="Anton Cermak">Anton Cermak</a> who was sitting alongside Roosevelt, but his attempt to murder Roosevelt failed when an alert spectator, Lillian Cross, hit his arm with her purse and deflected the bullet.<sup id="cite_ref-assassinationattempts_120-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-assassinationattempts-120"><span>[</span>118<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurns1956147_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurns1956147-131"><span>[</span>129<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132"><span>[</span>130<span>]</span></a></sup> Roosevelt leaned heavily on his "Brain Trust" of academic advisers, especially <a href="/wiki/Raymond_Moley" title="Raymond Moley">Raymond Moley</a>, when designing his policies; he offered cabinet positions to numerous candidates, but some declined. The cabinet member with the strongest independent base was <a href="/wiki/Cordell_Hull" title="Cordell Hull">Cordell Hull</a> at State. <a href="/wiki/William_Hartman_Woodin" title="William Hartman Woodin" class="mw-redirect">William Hartman Woodin</a>&#160;– at Treasury&#160;– was soon replaced by the much more powerful <a href="/wiki/Henry_Morgenthau,_Jr." title="Henry Morgenthau, Jr.">Henry Morgenthau, Jr.</a><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurns1956172_133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurns1956172-133"><span>[</span>131<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Presidency_.281933.E2.80.9345.29">Presidency (1933–45)</span></h2>
<p>Roosevelt appointed powerful men to top positions but made certain he made all the major decisions, regardless of delays, inefficiency or resentment. Analyzing the president's administrative style, historian <a href="/wiki/James_MacGregor_Burns" title="James MacGregor Burns">James MacGregor Burns</a> concludes:</p>
<dl>
<dd>The president stayed in charge of his administration...by drawing fully on his formal and informal powers as Chief Executive; by raising goals, creating momentum, inspiring a personal loyalty, getting the best out of people...by deliberately fostering among his aides a sense of competition and a clash of wills that led to disarray, heartbreak, and anger but also set off pulses of executive energy and sparks of creativity...by handing out one job to several men and several jobs to one man, thus strengthening his own position as a court of appeals, as a depository of information, and as a tool of co-ordination; by ignoring or bypassing collective decision-making agencies, such as the Cabinet...and always by persuading, flattering, juggling, improvising, reshuffling, harmonizing, conciliating, manipulating.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134"><span>[</span>132<span>]</span></a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="First_term.2C_1933.E2.80.9337">First term, 1933–37</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/New_Deal" title="New Deal">New Deal</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:FDR_Inauguration_1933.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/FDR_Inauguration_1933.jpg/170px-FDR_Inauguration_1933.jpg" width="170" height="212" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/FDR_Inauguration_1933.jpg/255px-FDR_Inauguration_1933.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/FDR_Inauguration_1933.jpg/340px-FDR_Inauguration_1933.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2790" data-file-height="3480" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:FDR_Inauguration_1933.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Hoover and Roosevelt on Inauguration Day, 1933.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>When Roosevelt was <a href="/wiki/First_inauguration_of_Franklin_D._Roosevelt" title="First inauguration of Franklin D. Roosevelt">inaugurated March&#160;4, 1933</a>, the U.S. was at the <a href="//en.wiktionary.org/wiki/nadir" class="extiw" title="wikt:nadir">nadir</a> of the worst depression in its history. A quarter of the workforce was unemployed. Farmers were in deep trouble as prices fell by 60%. Industrial production had fallen by more than half since 1929. Two million people were homeless. By the evening of March&#160;4, 32 of the 48 states&#160;– as well as the District of Columbia&#160;– had closed their banks.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135"><span>[</span>133<span>]</span></a></sup> The New York Federal Reserve Bank was unable to open on the 5th, as huge sums had been withdrawn by panicky customers in previous days.<sup id="cite_ref-SEK_136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SEK-136"><span>[</span>134<span>]</span></a></sup> Beginning with his inauguration address, Roosevelt began blaming the economic crisis on bankers and financiers, the quest for profit, and the self-interest basis of capitalism:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>Primarily this is because rulers of the exchange of mankind's goods have failed through their own stubbornness and their own incompetence, have admitted their failure, and have abdicated. Practices of the unscrupulous money changers stand indicted in the court of public opinion, rejected by the hearts and minds of men. True they have tried, but their efforts have been cast in the pattern of an outworn tradition. Faced by failure of credit they have proposed only the lending of more money. Stripped of the lure of profit by which to induce our people to follow their false leadership, they have resorted to exhortations, pleading tearfully for restored confidence... The money changers have fled from their high seats in the temple of our civilization. We may now restore that temple to the ancient truths. The measure of the restoration lies in the extent to which we apply social values more noble than mere monetary profit.<sup id="cite_ref-bartleby_137-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bartleby-137"><span>[</span>135<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Historians categorized Roosevelt's program as "relief, recovery and reform." Relief was urgently needed by tens of millions of unemployed. Recovery meant boosting the economy back to normal. Reform meant long-term fixes of what was wrong, especially with the financial and banking systems. Through Roosevelt's series of radio talks, known as <a href="/wiki/Fireside_chats" title="Fireside chats">fireside chats</a>, he presented his proposals directly to the American public.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurns1956167.E2.80.9368_138-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurns1956167.E2.80.9368-138"><span>[</span>136<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1934 FDR paid a visit to retired Justice <a href="/wiki/Oliver_Wendell_Holmes,_Jr." title="Oliver Wendell Holmes, Jr.">Oliver Wendell Holmes</a>, who mused about the President: "A second class intellect. But a first class temperament."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurns1956157_139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurns1956157-139"><span>[</span>137<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="First_New_Deal.2C_1933.E2.80.9334">First New Deal, 1933–34</span></h4>
<p>Roosevelt's "<a href="/wiki/Hundred_Days_Congress" title="Hundred Days Congress" class="mw-redirect">First 100 Days</a>" concentrated on the first part of his strategy: immediate relief. From March&#160;9 to June&#160;16, 1933, he sent Congress a record number of bills, all of which passed easily. To propose programs, Roosevelt relied on leading Senators such as <a href="/wiki/George_Norris" title="George Norris" class="mw-redirect">George Norris</a>, <a href="/wiki/Robert_F._Wagner" title="Robert F. Wagner">Robert F. Wagner</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hugo_Black" title="Hugo Black">Hugo Black</a>, as well as his <a href="/wiki/Brain_Trust" title="Brain Trust" class="mw-redirect">Brain Trust</a> of academic advisers. Like Hoover, he saw the Depression caused in part by people no longer spending or investing because they were afraid.</p>
<p>Roosevelt's inauguration on March 4, 1933, occurred in the middle of a <a href="/wiki/Bank_run" title="Bank run">bank panic</a>, hence the backdrop for his famous words: "The only thing we have to fear is fear itself."<sup id="cite_ref-bartleby_137-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bartleby-137"><span>[</span>135<span>]</span></a></sup> The very next day he declared a "bank holiday" and called for a special session of Congress to start March&#160;9, at which Congress passed the <a href="/wiki/Emergency_Banking_Act" title="Emergency Banking Act">Emergency Banking Act</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140"><span>[</span>138<span>]</span></a></sup> This was his first proposed step to recovery. To give Americans confidence in the banks, Roosevelt signed the <a href="/wiki/1933_Banking_Act" title="1933 Banking Act">Glass–Steagall Act</a> that created the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Deposit_Insurance_Corporation" title="Federal Deposit Insurance Corporation">Federal Deposit Insurance Corporation</a> (FDIC) to underwrite savings deposits.</p>
<table class="mbox-small" style="border:1px solid #aaa;background-color:#f9f9f9;">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image">
<div class="center">
<div class="floatnone"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/50px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png" width="50" height="50" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/75px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/100px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="160" data-file-height="160" /></div>

</td>
<td class="mbox-text plainlist" style="line-height:1.1em">
<div class="haudio">
<div style="padding:4px 0"><a href="/wiki/File:FDR_speech.ogg" title="File:FDR speech.ogg">Nothing to Fear</a></div>
<div>
<div class="mediaContainer" style="position:relative;display:block;width:220px"><audio id="mwe_player_0" style="width:220px" poster="/static/1.27.0-wmf.2/resources/assets/file-type-icons/fileicon-ogg.png" controls="" preload="none" class="kskin" data-durationhint="45.610666666667" data-startoffset="0" data-mwtitle="FDR_speech.ogg" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons"><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/7d/FDR_speech.ogg" type="audio/ogg; codecs=&quot;vorbis&quot;" data-title="Original Ogg file (125 kbps)" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="124981"></source>Sorry, your browser either has JavaScript disabled or does not have any supported player.<br />

<div class="description" style="padding:2px 0 0 0">Sample of the <i>Inaugural speech</i> from FDR</div>
</div>
<hr /></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td colspan="2" class="mbox-text" style="line-height:1.1em"><span><i>Problems playing this file? See <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Media_help" title="Wikipedia:Media help">media help</a>.</i></span></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>Relief measures included the continuation of Hoover's major relief program for the unemployed under its new name: <a href="/wiki/Federal_Emergency_Relief_Administration" title="Federal Emergency Relief Administration">Federal Emergency Relief Administration</a>. The most popular of all New Deal agencies&#160;– and Roosevelt's favorite&#160;– was the <a href="/wiki/Civilian_Conservation_Corps" title="Civilian Conservation Corps">Civilian Conservation Corps</a> (CCC), which hired 250,000 unemployed young men to work on rural local projects.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith2007319.E2.80.9322_141-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith2007319.E2.80.9322-141"><span>[</span>139<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Congress also gave the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Trade_Commission" title="Federal Trade Commission">Federal Trade Commission</a> broad new regulatory powers and provided mortgage relief to millions of farmers and homeowners.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith2007323_142-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith2007323-142"><span>[</span>140<span>]</span></a></sup> Roosevelt expanded a Hoover agency, the <a href="/wiki/Reconstruction_Finance_Corporation" title="Reconstruction Finance Corporation">Reconstruction Finance Corporation</a>, making it a major source of financing for railroads and industry. Roosevelt made agricultural relief a high priority and set up the first <a href="/wiki/Agricultural_Adjustment_Administration" title="Agricultural Adjustment Administration" class="mw-redirect">Agricultural Adjustment Administration</a> (AAA). The AAA tried to force higher prices for commodities by paying farmers to take land out of crops and to cut herds.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith2007318.E2.80.9319_143-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith2007318.E2.80.9319-143"><span>[</span>141<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Reform of the economy was the goal of the <a href="/wiki/National_Industrial_Recovery_Act" title="National Industrial Recovery Act">National Industrial Recovery Act</a> (NIRA) of 1933. It tried to end cutthroat competition by forcing industries to come up with codes that established the rules of operation for all firms within specific industries, such as minimum prices, agreements not to compete, and production restrictions. Industry leaders negotiated the codes which were approved by NIRA officials. Industry needed to raise wages as a condition for approval. Provisions encouraged unions and suspended <a href="/wiki/Anti-trust" title="Anti-trust" class="mw-redirect">anti-trust</a> laws. The NIRA was found to be unconstitutional by unanimous decision of the <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_the_United_States" title="Supreme Court of the United States">US Supreme Court</a> on May&#160;27, 1935. Roosevelt opposed the decision, saying, "The fundamental purposes and principles of the NIRA are sound. To abandon them is unthinkable. It would spell the return to industrial and labor chaos."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHawley1995124_144-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHawley1995124-144"><span>[</span>142<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1933, major new banking regulations were passed. In 1934, the <a href="/wiki/U.S._Securities_and_Exchange_Commission" title="U.S. Securities and Exchange Commission">Securities and Exchange Commission</a> was created to regulate Wall Street, with 1932 campaign fundraiser <a href="/wiki/Joseph_P._Kennedy" title="Joseph P. Kennedy" class="mw-redirect">Joseph P. Kennedy</a> in charge.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith2007346_145-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith2007346-145"><span>[</span>143<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Roosevelt wanted a federal minimum wage as part of the NIRA, arguing that. "No business which depends for existence on paying less than living wages to its workers has any right to continue in this country".<sup id="cite_ref-NYT-20140307_146-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20140307-146"><span>[</span>144<span>]</span></a></sup> The nation finally adopted the minimum wage in the <a href="/wiki/Fair_Labor_Standards_Act" title="Fair Labor Standards Act">Fair Labor Standards Act</a> of 1938, the last major domestic reform measure of the New Deal.<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147"><span>[</span>145<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Recovery was pursued through "pump-priming" (that is, federal spending).<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148"><span>[</span>146<span>]</span></a></sup> The NIRA included $3.3&#160;billion of spending through the <a href="/wiki/Public_Works_Administration" title="Public Works Administration">Public Works Administration</a> to stimulate the economy, which was to be handled by <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_the_Interior" title="United States Secretary of the Interior">Interior Secretary</a> <a href="/wiki/Harold_L._Ickes" title="Harold L. Ickes">Harold Ickes</a>. Roosevelt worked with Republican Senator <a href="/wiki/George_Norris" title="George Norris" class="mw-redirect">George Norris</a> to create the largest government-owned industrial enterprise in American history&#160;— the <a href="/wiki/Tennessee_Valley_Authority" title="Tennessee Valley Authority">Tennessee Valley Authority</a> (TVA)&#160;— which built dams and power stations, controlled floods, and modernized agriculture and home conditions in the poverty-stricken Tennessee Valley. The repeal of <a href="/wiki/Prohibition" title="Prohibition">prohibition</a> also brought in new tax revenues and helped Roosevelt keep a major campaign promise. <a href="/wiki/Executive_Order_6102" title="Executive Order 6102">Executive Order 6102</a> declared that all privately held gold of American citizens was to be sold to the US Treasury and the price raised from $20 to $35 per ounce. The goal was to counter the deflation which was paralyzing the economy.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFreidel1952.E2.80.93734:_320.E2.80.9339_149-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFreidel1952.E2.80.93734:_320.E2.80.9339-149"><span>[</span>147<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Roosevelt tried to keep his campaign promise by cutting the federal budget&#160;— including a reduction in military spending from $752&#160;million in 1932 to $531&#160;million in 1934 and a 40% cut in spending on veterans' benefits&#160;— by removing 500,000 veterans and widows from the pension rolls and reducing benefits for the remainder, as well as cutting the salaries of federal employees and reducing spending on research and education. But, the veterans were well organized and strongly protested; most benefits were restored or increased by 1934, but FDR vetoed their efforts to get a cash bonus.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFreidel1952.E2.80.93734:_448.E2.80.9352_150-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFreidel1952.E2.80.93734:_448.E2.80.9352-150"><span>[</span>148<span>]</span></a></sup> The benefit cuts also did not last. In June&#160;1933, Roosevelt restored $50 million in pension payments, and Congress added another $46 million more.<sup id="cite_ref-End_151-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-End-151"><span>[</span>149<span>]</span></a></sup> Veterans groups such as the <a href="/wiki/American_Legion" title="American Legion">American Legion</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Veterans_of_Foreign_Wars" title="Veterans of Foreign Wars">Veterans of Foreign Wars</a> won their campaign to transform their benefits from payments due in 1945 to immediate cash when Congress overrode the President's veto and passed the <a href="/wiki/Adjusted_Compensation_Payment_Act" title="Adjusted Compensation Payment Act">Bonus Act</a> in January&#160;1936.<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152"><span>[</span>150<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153"><span>[</span>151<span>]</span></a></sup> It pumped sums equal to 2% of the GDP into the consumer economy and had a major stimulus effect.<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154"><span>[</span>152<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Roosevelt also kept his promise to push for repeal of <a href="/wiki/Prohibition_in_the_United_States" title="Prohibition in the United States">Prohibition</a>. On March&#160;23, 1933, he signed the <a href="/wiki/Cullen%E2%80%93Harrison_Act" title="Cullen–Harrison Act">Cullen–Harrison Act</a> redefining 3.2% alcohol as the maximum allowed. That act was preceded by Congressional action in the drafting and passage of the <a href="/wiki/Twenty-first_Amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution" title="Twenty-first Amendment to the United States Constitution">21st Amendment</a>, which was ratified later that year.<sup id="cite_ref-Thornton_155-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thornton-155"><span>[</span>153<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Second_New_Deal.2C_1935.E2.80.9336">Second New Deal, 1935–36</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Signing_Of_The_Social_Security_Act.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Signing_Of_The_Social_Security_Act.jpg/220px-Signing_Of_The_Social_Security_Act.jpg" width="220" height="173" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Signing_Of_The_Social_Security_Act.jpg/330px-Signing_Of_The_Social_Security_Act.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Signing_Of_The_Social_Security_Act.jpg/440px-Signing_Of_The_Social_Security_Act.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4130" data-file-height="3240" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Signing_Of_The_Social_Security_Act.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Roosevelt signs the Social Security Act, August 14, 1935.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>After the 1934 Congressional elections, which gave Roosevelt large majorities in both houses, his administration drafted a fresh surge of New Deal legislation. These measures included the <a href="/wiki/Works_Progress_Administration" title="Works Progress Administration">Works Progress Administration</a> (WPA), which set up a national relief agency that employed two million family heads. At the height of WPA employment in 1938, unemployment was down from 20.6% in 1933 to only 12.5%, according to figures from Michael Darby.<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156"><span>[</span>154<span>]</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Social_Security_Act" title="Social Security Act">Social Security Act</a> established Social Security and promised economic security for the elderly, the poor and the sick. Senator <a href="/wiki/Robert_F._Wagner" title="Robert F. Wagner">Robert Wagner</a> wrote the <a href="/wiki/Wagner_Act" title="Wagner Act" class="mw-redirect">Wagner Act</a>, which officially became the <a href="/wiki/National_Labor_Relations_Act" title="National Labor Relations Act">National Labor Relations Act</a>. The act established the federal rights of workers to organize unions, to engage in <a href="/wiki/Collective_bargaining" title="Collective bargaining">collective bargaining</a>, and to take part in strikes.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:1936_FDR_%22Don%27t_Be_Fooled_by_Figures%22_Re-election_handbill.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/1936_FDR_%22Don%27t_Be_Fooled_by_Figures%22_Re-election_handbill.jpg/220px-1936_FDR_%22Don%27t_Be_Fooled_by_Figures%22_Re-election_handbill.jpg" width="220" height="347" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/1936_FDR_%22Don%27t_Be_Fooled_by_Figures%22_Re-election_handbill.jpg/330px-1936_FDR_%22Don%27t_Be_Fooled_by_Figures%22_Re-election_handbill.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/1936_FDR_%22Don%27t_Be_Fooled_by_Figures%22_Re-election_handbill.jpg/440px-1936_FDR_%22Don%27t_Be_Fooled_by_Figures%22_Re-election_handbill.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1433" data-file-height="2261" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:1936_FDR_%22Don%27t_Be_Fooled_by_Figures%22_Re-election_handbill.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
1936 re-election handbill for Roosevelt promoting his economic policy.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>While the First New Deal of 1933 had broad support from most sectors, the Second New Deal challenged the business community. Conservative Democrats, led by <a href="/wiki/Al_Smith" title="Al Smith">Al Smith</a>, fought back with the <a href="/wiki/American_Liberty_League" title="American Liberty League">American Liberty League</a>, savagely attacking Roosevelt and equating him with <a href="/wiki/Karl_Marx" title="Karl Marx">Karl Marx</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin" title="Vladimir Lenin">Vladimir Lenin</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Fried_157-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fried-157"><span>[</span>155<span>]</span></a></sup> But Smith overplayed his hand, and his boisterous rhetoric let Roosevelt isolate his opponents and identify them with the wealthy vested interests that opposed the New Deal, strengthening Roosevelt for the 1936 landslide.<sup id="cite_ref-Fried_157-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fried-157"><span>[</span>155<span>]</span></a></sup> By contrast, the labor unions, energized by the Wagner Act, signed up millions of new members and became a major backer of Roosevelt's reelections in 1936, 1940 and 1944.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurns1956350_158-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurns1956350-158"><span>[</span>156<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Larry_Schweikart" title="Larry Schweikart">Larry Schweikart</a> and Michael Allen disagree with the prevailing belief that there were two New Deals in the Roosevelt administration.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchweikartAllen2004559_159-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchweikartAllen2004559-159"><span>[</span>157<span>]</span></a></sup> They argue that there is no evidence of any such blueprint for Roosevelt's programs, and that abundant evidence shows FDR's policies were formulated and executed haphazardly, fluctuating in the hands of a revolving cast of presidential advisors.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchweikartAllen2004560_160-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchweikartAllen2004560-160"><span>[</span>158<span>]</span></a></sup> Biographer James M. Burns suggests that Roosevelt's policy decisions were guided more by pragmatism than ideology, and that he "was like the general of a <a href="/wiki/Guerrilla" title="Guerrilla" class="mw-redirect">guerrilla</a> army whose columns, fighting blindly in the mountains through dense ravines and thickets, suddenly converge, half by plan and half by coincidence, and debouch into the plain below."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurns1956226_161-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurns1956226-161"><span>[</span>159<span>]</span></a></sup> Roosevelt argued that such apparently haphazard methodology was necessary. "The country needs and, unless I mistake its temper, the country demands bold, persistent experimentation," he wrote. "It is common sense to take a method and try it; if it fails, admit it frankly and try another. But above all, try something."<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162"><span>[</span>160<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Economic_environment">Economic environment</span></h4>
<div class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Great_Depression_in_the_United_States#Effects" title="Great Depression in the United States">Great Depression in the United States § Effects</a></div>
<p>Government spending increased from 8.0% of gross national product (GNP) <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Hoover#Taxes.2C_revenues.2C_and_deficits" title="Herbert Hoover">under Hoover</a> in 1932 to 10.2% of the GNP in 1936. The <a href="/wiki/National_debt" title="National debt" class="mw-redirect">national debt</a> as a percentage of the GNP had more than doubled under Hoover from 16% to 40% of the GNP in early 1933. It held steady at close to 40% as late as fall 1941, then grew rapidly during the war.<sup id="cite_ref-Historical_Statistics_1976_163-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Historical_Statistics_1976-163"><span>[</span>161<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:312px;"><a href="/wiki/File:US_Unemployment_1910-1960.gif" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/US_Unemployment_1910-1960.gif/310px-US_Unemployment_1910-1960.gif" width="310" height="205" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/US_Unemployment_1910-1960.gif/465px-US_Unemployment_1910-1960.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/58/US_Unemployment_1910-1960.gif 2x" data-file-width="620" data-file-height="410" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:US_Unemployment_1910-1960.gif" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Unemployment rate in the U.S. 1910–60, with the years of the Great Depression (1929–39) highlighted.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Deficit spending had been recommended by some economists, most notably by <a href="/wiki/John_Maynard_Keynes" title="John Maynard Keynes">John Maynard Keynes</a> of Britain. The GNP was 34% higher in 1936 than in 1932 and 58% higher in 1940 on the eve of war. That is, the economy grew 58% from 1932 to 1940 in 8 years of peacetime, and then grew 56% from 1940 to 1945 in 5 years of wartime.<sup id="cite_ref-Historical_Statistics_1976_163-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Historical_Statistics_1976-163"><span>[</span>161<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Unemployment fell dramatically in Roosevelt's first term, from 25% when he took office to 14.3% in 1937. However, it increased slightly to 19.0% in 1938 ("a depression within a depression") and fell to 17.2% in 1939, and then dropped again to 14.6% in 1940 until it reached 1.9% in 1945 during World War II.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmiley1983487.E2.80.9393_164-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmiley1983487.E2.80.9393-164"><span>[</span>162<span>]</span></a></sup> Total employment during Roosevelt's term expanded by 18.31&#160;million jobs, with an average annual increase in jobs during his administration of 5.3%.<sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165"><span>[</span>163<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166"><span>[</span>164<span>]</span></a></sup> Roosevelt considered his New Deal policies as central to his legacy, and in his 1944 <a href="/wiki/State_of_the_Union_Address" title="State of the Union Address" class="mw-redirect">State of the Union Address</a>, he advocated that Americans should think of basic economic rights as a <a href="/wiki/Second_Bill_of_Rights" title="Second Bill of Rights">Second Bill of Rights</a>.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
<div id="mwe_player_1" class="PopUpMediaTransform" style="width:220px;" videopayload="&lt;div class=&quot;mediaContainer&quot; style=&quot;position:relative;display:block;width:320px&quot;&gt;&lt;video id=&quot;mwe_player_2&quot;
