<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Second_Bill_of_Rights_Speech.ogv" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Franklin Delano Roosevelt announced the plan for a <a href="/wiki/Second_Bill_of_Rights" title="Second Bill of Rights">bill of social and economic rights</a> in the <a href="/wiki/State_of_the_Union_address" title="State of the Union address" class="mw-redirect">State of the Union address</a> broadcast on January&#160;11, 1944. (excerpt)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Roosevelt did not raise income taxes before World War II began; however <a href="/wiki/Payroll_tax" title="Payroll tax">payroll taxes</a> were introduced to fund the new <a href="/wiki/Social_Security_(United_States)" title="Social Security (United States)">Social Security</a> program in 1937. He also convinced Congress to spend more on many various programs never before seen in American history. Under the revenue pressures brought on by the depression, most states added or increased taxes, including sales as well as income taxes. Roosevelt's proposal for new taxes on corporate savings were highly controversial in 1936–37, and were rejected by Congress. During the war he pushed for even higher income tax rates for individuals (reaching a marginal tax rate of 91%) and corporations and a cap on high salaries for executives. He also issued Executive Order 9250 in October&#160;1942, later to be rescinded by Congress, which raised the marginal tax rate for salaries exceeding $25,000 (after tax) to 100%, thereby limiting salaries to $25,000 (about $361,000 today).<sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167"><span>[</span>165<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168"><span>[</span>166<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169"><span>[</span>167<span>]</span></a></sup> To fund the war, Congress not only broadened the base so that almost every employee paid federal income taxes, but also introduced <a href="/wiki/Withholding_taxes" title="Withholding taxes" class="mw-redirect">withholding taxes</a> in 1943.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Foreign_policy.2C_1933.E2.80.9337">Foreign policy, 1933–37</span></h4>
<p>The rejection of the <a href="/wiki/League_of_Nations" title="League of Nations">League of Nations</a> treaty in 1919 marked the dominance of <a href="/wiki/Isolationism" title="Isolationism">isolationism</a> from world organizations in American foreign policy. Despite Roosevelt's Wilsonian background, he and <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_State" title="United States Secretary of State">Secretary of State</a> <a href="/wiki/Cordell_Hull" title="Cordell Hull">Cordell Hull</a> acted with great care not to provoke isolationist sentiment. Roosevelt's "bombshell" message to the <a href="/wiki/London_Economic_Conference" title="London Economic Conference">world monetary conference</a> in 1933 effectively ended any major efforts by the world powers to collaborate on ending the worldwide depression, and allowed Roosevelt a free hand in economic policy.<sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170"><span>[</span>168<span>]</span></a></sup> Roosevelt was a lifelong free-trader and anti-imperialist. Ending European <a href="/wiki/Colonialism" title="Colonialism">colonialism</a> was one of his objectives.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJustusStoler2005120_171-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJustusStoler2005120-171"><span>[</span>169<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The main foreign policy initiative of Roosevelt's first term was the <a href="/wiki/Good_Neighbor_Policy" title="Good Neighbor Policy" class="mw-redirect">Good Neighbor Policy</a>, which was a re-evaluation of U.S. policy towards <a href="/wiki/Latin_America" title="Latin America">Latin America</a>. Since the <a href="/wiki/Monroe_Doctrine" title="Monroe Doctrine">Monroe Doctrine</a> of 1823, this area had been seen as an American <a href="/wiki/Sphere_of_influence" title="Sphere of influence">sphere of influence</a>. American forces were <a href="/wiki/United_States_occupation_of_Haiti" title="United States occupation of Haiti">withdrawn</a> from Haiti, and new treaties with Cuba and Panama ended their status as U.S. <a href="/wiki/Protectorate" title="Protectorate">protectorates</a>. In December&#160;1933, Roosevelt signed the <a href="/wiki/Montevideo_Convention" title="Montevideo Convention">Montevideo Convention</a> on the Rights and Duties of States, renouncing the right to intervene unilaterally in the affairs of Latin American countries.<sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172"><span>[</span>170<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The isolationist movement was bolstered in the early to mid-1930s by U.S. Senator <a href="/wiki/Gerald_Nye" title="Gerald Nye">Gerald Nye</a> and others who succeeded in their effort to stop the "merchants of death" in the U.S. from selling arms abroad.<sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173"><span>[</span>171<span>]</span></a></sup> This effort took the form of the <a href="/wiki/Neutrality_Acts_of_1930s" title="Neutrality Acts of 1930s">Neutrality Acts</a>; the president asked for, but was refused, a provision to give him the discretion to allow the sale of arms to victims of aggression.<sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174"><span>[</span>172<span>]</span></a></sup> In the interim, Italy under <a href="/wiki/Benito_Mussolini" title="Benito Mussolini">Benito Mussolini</a> proceeded to <a href="/wiki/Second_Italo-Abyssinian_War" title="Second Italo-Abyssinian War" class="mw-redirect">overcome</a> <a href="/wiki/Ethiopian_Empire" title="Ethiopian Empire">Ethiopia</a>, and the Italians joined <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Germany" title="Nazi Germany">Nazi Germany</a> in supporting the General Franco and the Nationalist cause in the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Civil_War" title="Spanish Civil War">Spanish Civil War</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175"><span>[</span>173<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1936 Germany and Japan signed a <a href="/wiki/Anti-Comintern_Pact" title="Anti-Comintern Pact">Anti-Comintern Pact</a>, but they never coordinated their strategies.<sup id="cite_ref-176" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-176"><span>[</span>174<span>]</span></a></sup> Congress passed, and the president signed, a mandatory arms embargo at a time when dictators in Europe and Asia were girding for world war.<sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177"><span>[</span>175<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Landslide_re-election.2C_1936">Landslide re-election, 1936</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1936" title="United States presidential election, 1936">United States presidential election, 1936</a></div>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1936" title="United States presidential election, 1936">1936 presidential election</a>, Roosevelt campaigned on his New Deal programs against <a href="/wiki/Kansas" title="Kansas">Kansas</a> Governor <a href="/wiki/Alf_Landon" title="Alf Landon">Alf Landon</a>, who accepted much of the New Deal but objected that it was hostile to business and involved too much waste. Roosevelt and Garner won 60.8% of the vote and carried every state except <a href="/wiki/Maine" title="Maine">Maine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vermont" title="Vermont">Vermont</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178"><span>[</span>176<span>]</span></a></sup> The New Deal Democrats won even larger majorities in Congress. Roosevelt was backed by a coalition of voters which included traditional Democrats across the country, small farmers, the "<a href="/wiki/Solid_South" title="Solid South">Solid South</a>" (mostly white Democrats), Catholics, big city political machines, labor unions, northern African Americans (southern ones were still disfranchised), Jews, intellectuals and political liberals. This coalition, frequently referred to as the <a href="/wiki/New_Deal_coalition" title="New Deal coalition">New Deal coalition</a>, remained largely intact for the Democratic Party until the 1960s.<sup id="cite_ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179"><span>[</span>177<span>]</span></a></sup> Roosevelt's popularity generated massive volumes of correspondence that had to be responded to. He once told his son James, "Two short sentences will generally answer any known letter."<sup id="cite_ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180"><span>[</span>178<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Second_term.2C_1937.E2.80.9341">Second term, 1937–41</span></h3>
<p>In contrast to his first term, little major legislation was passed during Roosevelt's second term. There was the <a href="/wiki/Housing_Act_of_1937" title="Housing Act of 1937">Housing Act of 1937</a>, a second Agricultural Adjustment Act, and the <a href="/wiki/Fair_Labor_Standards_Act" title="Fair Labor Standards Act">Fair Labor Standards Act</a> (FLSA) of 1938, which created the <a href="/wiki/Minimum_wage" title="Minimum wage">minimum wage</a>. When the economy began to deteriorate again in late 1937, Roosevelt asked Congress for $5&#160;billion in WPA relief and public works funding. This managed to eventually create as many as 3.3 million WPA jobs by 1938. Projects accomplished under the WPA ranged from new federal courthouses and post offices, to facilities and infrastructure for national parks, bridges and other infrastructure across the country, and architectural surveys and archeological excavations&#160;— investments to construct facilities and preserve important resources. Beyond this, however, Roosevelt recommended to a special congressional session only a permanent national farm act, administrative reorganization and regional planning measures, which were leftovers from a regular session. According to Burns, this attempt illustrated Roosevelt's inability to decide on a basic economic program.<sup id="cite_ref-181" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-181"><span>[</span>179<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_the_United_States" title="Supreme Court of the United States">Supreme Court</a> became Roosevelt's primary focus during his second term, after the court overturned many of his programs. In particular in 1935, the Court unanimously ruled that the <a href="/wiki/National_Recovery_Act" title="National Recovery Act" class="mw-redirect">National Recovery Act</a> (NRA) was an unconstitutional delegation of legislative power to the president. Roosevelt stunned Congress in early 1937 by proposing a law to allow him to appoint up to six new justices, what he referred to as a "persistent infusion of new blood."<sup id="cite_ref-182" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-182"><span>[</span>180<span>]</span></a></sup> This "<a href="/wiki/Court_packing" title="Court packing" class="mw-redirect">court packing</a>" plan ran into intense political opposition from his own party, led by Vice President Garner, since it upset the separation of powers and gave the President control over the Court. Roosevelt's proposal to expand the court failed;<sup id="cite_ref-183" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-183"><span>[</span>181<span>]</span></a></sup> but by 1941, Roosevelt had appointed eight of the nine justices of the court, a change in membership which resulted in a court that began to ratify his policies.<sup id="cite_ref-184" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-184"><span>[</span>182<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185"><span>[</span>183<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Roosevelt at first had massive support from the rapidly growing labor unions, but they split into bitterly feuding <a href="/wiki/American_Federation_of_Labor" title="American Federation of Labor">AFL</a> and <a href="/wiki/Congress_of_Industrial_Organizations" title="Congress of Industrial Organizations">CIO</a> factions, the latter led by <a href="/wiki/John_L._Lewis" title="John L. Lewis">John L. Lewis</a>. Roosevelt pronounced a "plague on both your houses," but labor's disunity weakened the party in the elections from 1938 through 1946.<sup id="cite_ref-186" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-186"><span>[</span>184<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Determined to overcome the opposition of conservative Democrats in Congress (mostly from the South), Roosevelt became involved in the 1938 Democratic primaries, actively campaigning for challengers who were more supportive of New Deal reform. His targets denounced Roosevelt for trying to take over the Democratic party and to win reelection, using the argument that they were independent. Roosevelt failed badly, managing to defeat only one target, a conservative Democrat from <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-187" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-187"><span>[</span>185<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In the November&#160;1938 election, Democrats lost six Senate seats and 71 House seats. Losses were concentrated among pro-New Deal Democrats. When Congress reconvened in 1939, Republicans under Senator <a href="/wiki/Robert_A._Taft" title="Robert A. Taft">Robert Taft</a> formed a <a href="/wiki/Conservative_coalition" title="Conservative coalition">Conservative coalition</a> with Southern Democrats, virtually ending Roosevelt's ability to get his domestic proposals enacted into law. The minimum wage law of 1938 was the last substantial New Deal reform act passed by Congress.<sup id="cite_ref-188" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-188"><span>[</span>186<span>]</span></a></sup> Following the autumn Congressional elections in 1938, Congress was now dominated by conservatives, many of whom feared that Roosevelt was "aiming at a dictatorship," according to the historian Hugh Brogan.<sup id="cite_ref-189" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-189"><span>[</span>187<span>]</span></a></sup> In addition, as noted by another historian, after the 1938 election increased the strength of Republicans,</p>
<dl>
<dd>"conservative Democrats held the balance of power between liberals and Republicans, and they used it to prevent completion of the structure of the Second New Deal."<sup id="cite_ref-Goldberg2007P133_190-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Goldberg2007P133-190"><span>[</span>188<span>]</span></a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<p>Roosevelt had always belonged to the more liberal wing of the Democratic Party. He sought a realignment that would solidify liberal dominance by means of landslides in 1932, 1934 and 1936. During the 1932 campaign he predicted privately, "I'll be in the White House for eight years. When those years are over, there'll be a Progressive party. It may not be Democratic, but it will be Progressive." When the third consecutive landslide in 1936 failed to produce major legislation in 1937, his recourse was to purge his conservative opponents in 1938.<sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191"><span>[</span>189<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Foreign_policy.2C_1937.E2.80.9341">Foreign policy, 1937–41</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Quezon_Roosevelt.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Quezon_Roosevelt.jpg/220px-Quezon_Roosevelt.jpg" width="220" height="175" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Quezon_Roosevelt.jpg/330px-Quezon_Roosevelt.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Quezon_Roosevelt.jpg/440px-Quezon_Roosevelt.jpg 2x" data-file-width="541" data-file-height="430" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Quezon_Roosevelt.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Roosevelt welcomes <a href="/wiki/Manuel_L._Quezon" title="Manuel L. Quezon">Manuel L. Quezon</a>, the second President of the <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">Philippines</a> and his family in Washington, May 13, 1942.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The aggressive foreign policy of Nazi dictator <a href="/wiki/Adolf_Hitler" title="Adolf Hitler">Adolf Hitler</a> in Germany after 1933 aroused fears of a new world war. Americans wanted to keep out of it and in 1937 Congress passed an even more stringent Neutrality act. But when <a href="/wiki/Second_Sino-Japanese_War" title="Second Sino-Japanese War">Japan invaded China</a> in 1937, public opinion strongly favored China, and Roosevelt found various ways to assist that nation.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDallek1995273_192-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDallek1995273-192"><span>[</span>190<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In October 1937, Roosevelt gave the <a href="/wiki/Quarantine_Speech" title="Quarantine Speech">Quarantine Speech</a> aiming to contain aggressor nations. He proposed that warmongering states be treated as a public health menace and be "quarantined."<sup id="cite_ref-quarantine_193-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-quarantine-193"><span>[</span>191<span>]</span></a></sup> Meanwhile, he secretly stepped up a program to build long-range submarines that could blockade Japan.<sup id="cite_ref-194" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-194"><span>[</span>192<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>At the time of the <a href="/wiki/Munich_Agreement" title="Munich Agreement">Munich Agreement</a> in 1938&#160;— with the U.S. not represented&#160;— Roosevelt said the country would not join a "stop-Hitler bloc" under any circumstances. He made it quite clear that, in the event of German aggression against Czechoslovakia, the U.S. would remain neutral.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdamthwaite1977209_195-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdamthwaite1977209-195"><span>[</span>193<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDallek1995166.E2.80.9373_196-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDallek1995166.E2.80.9373-196"><span>[</span>194<span>]</span></a></sup> Roosevelt said in 1939 that France and Britain were America's "first line of defense" and needed American aid, but because of widespread isolationist sentiment, he reiterated the US itself would not go to war.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWatt1989134.E2.80.9336_197-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWatt1989134.E2.80.9336-197"><span>[</span>195<span>]</span></a></sup> In the spring of 1939, Roosevelt allowed the French to place huge orders with the American aircraft industry on a cash-and-carry basis, as allowed by law. Most of the aircraft ordered had not arrived in France by the time of its collapse in May&#160;1940, so Roosevelt arranged in June 1940 for French orders to be sold to the British.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKeylor1998233.E2.80.9344_198-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKeylor1998233.E2.80.9344-198"><span>[</span>196<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>When <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> began in September 1939 with Germany's invasion of Poland, Roosevelt rejected the Wilsonian neutrality stance and sought ways to assist Britain and France militarily.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlack2005503.E2.80.936_199-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlack2005503.E2.80.936-199"><span>[</span>197<span>]</span></a></sup> At first he gave only covert support to repeal of the arms embargo provisions of the Neutrality Act.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurns1956396_200-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurns1956396-200"><span>[</span>198<span>]</span></a></sup> He began a regular secret correspondence with the First Lord of the Admiralty <a href="/wiki/Winston_Churchill" title="Winston Churchill">Winston Churchill</a> in September&#160;1939&#160;— the first of 1,700 letters and telegrams between them<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGunther195015_201-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGunther195015-201"><span>[</span>199<span>]</span></a></sup>&#160;— discussing ways of supporting Britain. Roosevelt forged a close personal relationship with Churchill, who became <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Prime Minister of the United Kingdom">Prime Minister of the United Kingdom</a> in May 1940.</p>
<p>His relations with <a href="/wiki/Charles_de_Gaulle" title="Charles de Gaulle">Charles de Gaulle</a>, leader of <a href="/wiki/Free_France" title="Free France">Free France</a>, were more strained, for a long time he refused to recognize de Gaulle as the representative of France, preferring to deal with representatives of the <a href="/wiki/Vichy_regime" title="Vichy regime" class="mw-redirect">Vichy government</a>. Roosevelt did not recognize <a href="/wiki/Provisional_Government_of_the_French_Republic" title="Provisional Government of the French Republic">de Gaulle's provisional government</a> until late 1944.<sup id="cite_ref-202" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-202"><span>[</span>200<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In April 1940, Germany invaded Denmark and Norway, followed by invasions of the Netherlands, Belgium, Luxembourg and France in May. The German victories left Britain isolated in western Europe. Roosevelt, who was determined that Britain not be defeated, took advantage of the rapid shifts of public opinion. The fall of Paris shocked American opinion, and isolationist sentiment declined. A consensus was clear that military spending had to be dramatically expanded. There was no consensus on how much the US should risk war in helping Britain.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeuchtenberg1963399.E2.80.93402_203-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeuchtenberg1963399.E2.80.93402-203"><span>[</span>201<span>]</span></a></sup> In July&#160;1940, FDR appointed two interventionist Republican leaders, <a href="/wiki/Henry_L._Stimson" title="Henry L. Stimson">Henry L. Stimson</a> and <a href="/wiki/Frank_Knox" title="Frank Knox">Frank Knox</a>, as Secretaries of War and the Navy, respectively. Both parties gave support to his plans for a rapid build-up the American military, but the isolationists warned that Roosevelt would get the nation into an unnecessary war with Germany.<sup id="cite_ref-204" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-204"><span>[</span>202<span>]</span></a></sup> Congress authorized the nation's first peacetime draft.<sup id="cite_ref-205" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-205"><span>[</span>203<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:US_President_Franklin_Roosevelt_Presidential_Trips.PNG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/US_President_Franklin_Roosevelt_Presidential_Trips.PNG/220px-US_President_Franklin_Roosevelt_Presidential_Trips.PNG" width="220" height="96" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/US_President_Franklin_Roosevelt_Presidential_Trips.PNG/330px-US_President_Franklin_Roosevelt_Presidential_Trips.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/US_President_Franklin_Roosevelt_Presidential_Trips.PNG/440px-US_President_Franklin_Roosevelt_Presidential_Trips.PNG 2x" data-file-width="1425" data-file-height="625" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:US_President_Franklin_Roosevelt_Presidential_Trips.PNG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Foreign trips of Roosevelt during his presidency.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Roosevelt used his personal charisma to build support for intervention. America should be the "<a href="/wiki/Arsenal_of_Democracy" title="Arsenal of Democracy">Arsenal of Democracy</a>", he told his fireside audience.<sup id="cite_ref-206" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-206"><span>[</span>204<span>]</span></a></sup> On September&#160;2, 1940, Roosevelt openly defied the Neutrality Acts by passing the <a href="/wiki/Destroyers_for_Bases_Agreement" title="Destroyers for Bases Agreement">Destroyers for Bases Agreement</a>, which, in exchange for military base rights in the British Caribbean Islands, gave 50 WWI American <a href="/wiki/Destroyer" title="Destroyer">destroyers</a> to Britain. The U.S. also received free base rights in <a href="/wiki/Bermuda" title="Bermuda">Bermuda</a> and <a href="/wiki/Newfoundland_(island)" title="Newfoundland (island)">Newfoundland</a>, allowing British forces to be moved to the sharper end of the war; the idea of an exchange of warships for bases such as these originated in the cabinet.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurns1956438_207-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurns1956438-207"><span>[</span>205<span>]</span></a></sup> Hitler and Mussolini responded to the deal by joining with Japan in the <a href="/wiki/Tripartite_Pact" title="Tripartite Pact">Tripartite Pact</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurns1956441_208-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurns1956441-208"><span>[</span>206<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The agreement with Britain was a precursor of the March&#160;1941 Lend-Lease agreement, which began to direct massive military and economic aid to Britain, the Republic of China, and later the Soviet Union. For foreign policy advice, Roosevelt turned to <a href="/wiki/Harry_Hopkins" title="Harry Hopkins">Harry Hopkins</a>, who became his chief wartime advisor. They sought innovative ways short of going to war to help Britain, whose financial resources were exhausted by the end of 1940.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurns197044_209-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurns197044-209"><span>[</span>207<span>]</span></a></sup> Congress, where isolationist sentiment was waning, passed the <a href="/wiki/Lend-Lease_Act" title="Lend-Lease Act" class="mw-redirect">Lend-Lease Act</a> in March&#160;1941, allowing the U.S. to give Wales, England, Scotland, China, and later the Soviet Union military supplies. The legislation had hit a logjam until Senators Byrd, Byrnes and Taft added a provision subjecting it to appropriation by Congress.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurns197049_210-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurns197049-210"><span>[</span>208<span>]</span></a></sup> Congress voted to commit to spend $50&#160;billion on military supplies from 1941 to 1945. In sharp contrast to the loans of <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>, there would be no repayment after the war. Until late in 1941, Roosevelt refused Churchill's urgent requests for armed escort of ships bound for Britain, insisting on a more passive patrolling function in the western Atlantic.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurns197095_211-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurns197095-211"><span>[</span>209<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Election_of_1940">Election of 1940</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1940" title="United States presidential election, 1940">United States presidential election, 1940</a></div>
<p>The two-term tradition had been an unwritten rule (until the <a href="/wiki/Twenty-second_Amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution" title="Twenty-second Amendment to the United States Constitution">22nd Amendment</a> after Roosevelt's presidency) since George Washington declined to run for a third term in 1796. Both <a href="/wiki/Ulysses_S._Grant" title="Ulysses S. Grant">Ulysses S. Grant</a> and <a href="/wiki/Theodore_Roosevelt" title="Theodore Roosevelt">Theodore Roosevelt</a> were attacked for trying to obtain a third non-consecutive term. Roosevelt systematically undercut prominent Democrats who were angling for the nomination, including Vice President John Nance Garner<sup id="cite_ref-212" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-212"><span>[</span>210<span>]</span></a></sup> and two cabinet members, Secretary of State <a href="/wiki/Cordell_Hull" title="Cordell Hull">Cordell Hull</a> and <a href="/wiki/James_Farley" title="James Farley">James Farley</a>, Roosevelt's campaign manager in 1932 and 1936, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Postmaster_General" title="United States Postmaster General">Postmaster General</a> and the Democratic Party chairman. Roosevelt moved the convention to Chicago where he had strong support from the city machine (which controlled the auditorium sound system). At the convention the opposition was poorly organized, but Farley had packed the galleries. Roosevelt sent a message saying that he would not run unless he was drafted, and that the delegates were free to vote for anyone. The delegates were stunned; then the loudspeaker screamed "We want Roosevelt... The world wants Roosevelt!" The delegates went wild and he was nominated by 946 to 147 on the first ballot. The tactic employed by Roosevelt was not entirely successful, as his goal had been to be drafted by acclamation.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurns1956428_213-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurns1956428-213"><span>[</span>211<span>]</span></a></sup> The new vice-presidential nominee was <a href="/wiki/Henry_A._Wallace" title="Henry A. Wallace">Henry Agard Wallace</a>, a liberal intellectual who was Secretary of Agriculture.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurns1956408.E2.80.9315.2C_422.E2.80.9330_214-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurns1956408.E2.80.9315.2C_422.E2.80.9330-214"><span>[</span>212<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In his campaign against Republican <a href="/wiki/Wendell_Willkie" title="Wendell Willkie">Wendell Willkie</a>, Roosevelt stressed both his proven leadership experience and his intention to do everything possible to keep the United States out of war. In one of his speeches he declared to potential recruits that "you boys are not going to be sent into any foreign war."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurns19706_215-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurns19706-215"><span>[</span>213<span>]</span></a></sup> He won the <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1940" title="United States presidential election, 1940">1940 election</a> with 55% of the popular vote and 38 of the 48 states, and thus winning almost 85% of the electoral vote (449 to 82).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurns1956454_216-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurns1956454-216"><span>[</span>214<span>]</span></a></sup> A shift to the left within the Administration was shown by the naming of Henry A. Wallace as Vice President in place of the conservative Texan John Nance Garner, who had become a bitter enemy of Roosevelt after 1937.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Third_term.2C_1941.E2.80.9345">Third term, 1941–45</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/United_States_home_front_during_World_War_II" title="United States home front during World War II">United States home front during World War II</a></div>
<p>Roosevelt tried to avoid repeating what he saw as Woodrow Wilson's mistakes in World War I.<sup id="cite_ref-217" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-217"><span>[</span>215<span>]</span></a></sup> He often made exactly the opposite decision. Wilson called for neutrality in thought and deed, while Roosevelt made it clear his administration strongly favored Britain and China. Unlike the loans in World War I, the United States made large-scale grants of military and economic aid to the Allies through <a href="/wiki/Lend-lease" title="Lend-lease" class="mw-redirect">Lend-Lease</a>, with little expectation of repayment. Wilson did not greatly expand war production before the declaration of war; Roosevelt did. Wilson waited for the declaration to begin a draft; Roosevelt started one in 1940. Wilson never made the United States an official ally but Roosevelt did. Wilson never met with the top Allied leaders but Roosevelt did. Wilson proclaimed independent policy, as seen in the 14 Points, while Roosevelt always had a collaborative policy with the Allies. In 1917, United States declared war on Germany; in 1941, Roosevelt waited until the enemy attacked at Pearl Harbor. Wilson refused to collaborate with the Republicans; Roosevelt named leading Republicans to head the War Department and the Navy Department. Wilson let General George Pershing make the major military decisions; Roosevelt made the major decisions in his war. He rejected the idea of an armistice and demanded unconditional surrender. Roosevelt often mentioned his role in the Wilson administration, but added that he had profited more from Wilson's errors than from his successes.<sup id="cite_ref-218" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-218"><span>[</span>216<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-219" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-219"><span>[</span>217<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-220" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-220"><span>[</span>218<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-221" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-221"><span>[</span>219<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Policies">Policies</span></h4>
<table class="mbox-small" style="border:1px solid #aaa;background-color:#f9f9f9;">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image">
<div class="center">
<div class="floatnone"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/50px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png" width="50" height="50" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/75px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/100px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="160" data-file-height="160" /></div>
</div>
</td>
<td class="mbox-text plainlist" style="line-height:1.1em">
