<p>President Roosevelt appointed eight Justices to the <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_the_United_States" title="Supreme Court of the United States">Supreme Court of the United States</a>, more than any other President except George Washington, who appointed ten. By 1941, eight of the nine Justices were Roosevelt appointees. Harlan Fiske Stone was elevated to Chief Justice from the position of Associate Justice by Roosevelt.</p>
<p>Roosevelt's appointees would not share ideologies, and some, like Hugo Black and Felix Frankfurter, would become "lifelong adversaries."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBall20069_282-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBall20069-282"><span>[</span>278<span>]</span></a></sup> Frankfurter even labeled his more liberal colleagues Rutledge, Murphy, Black, and Douglas as part of an "Axis" of opposition to his <a href="/wiki/Judicial_restraint" title="Judicial restraint">judicial restraint</a> agenda.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBall200614_283-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBall200614-283"><span>[</span>279<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Civil_rights">Civil rights</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Franklin_D._Roosevelt%27s_record_on_civil_rights" title="Franklin D. Roosevelt's record on civil rights">Franklin D. Roosevelt's record on civil rights</a></div>
<p>Roosevelt was a hero to major minority groups, especially African Americans, Catholics, and Jews, and was highly successful in attracting large majorities of these voters into his <a href="/wiki/New_Deal_coalition" title="New Deal coalition">New Deal coalition</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-284" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-284"><span>[</span>280<span>]</span></a></sup> He won strong support from Chinese Americans and Filipino Americans, but not Japanese Americans, as he was responsible for their losses due to internship in concentration camps during the war.<sup id="cite_ref-285" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-285"><span>[</span>281<span>]</span></a></sup> Roosevelt's understanding and awareness of problems in the world of the American Indians was questioned during the Hopi Hearings which were held in 1955.</p>
<p>African Americans and <a href="/wiki/Native_Americans_in_the_United_States" title="Native Americans in the United States">Native Americans</a> fared well in two New Deal relief programs, the <a href="/wiki/Civilian_Conservation_Corps" title="Civilian Conservation Corps">Civilian Conservation Corps</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Reorganization_Act" title="Indian Reorganization Act">Indian Reorganization Act</a>, respectively. Sitkoff reported that the WPA "provided an economic floor for the whole black community in the 1930s, rivaling both agriculture and domestic service as the chief source" of income.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESitkoff197871_286-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESitkoff197871-286"><span>[</span>282<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:(Mary_McLeod_Bethune),_%22Mrs._Eleanor_Roosevelt_and_others_at_the_opening_of_Midway_Hall,_one_of_two_residence_halls_buil_-_NARA_-_533032.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/%28Mary_McLeod_Bethune%29%2C_%22Mrs._Eleanor_Roosevelt_and_others_at_the_opening_of_Midway_Hall%2C_one_of_two_residence_halls_buil_-_NARA_-_533032.jpg/220px-%28Mary_McLeod_Bethune%29%2C_%22Mrs._Eleanor_Roosevelt_and_others_at_the_opening_of_Midway_Hall%2C_one_of_two_residence_halls_buil_-_NARA_-_533032.jpg" width="220" height="174" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/%28Mary_McLeod_Bethune%29%2C_%22Mrs._Eleanor_Roosevelt_and_others_at_the_opening_of_Midway_Hall%2C_one_of_two_residence_halls_buil_-_NARA_-_533032.jpg/330px-%28Mary_McLeod_Bethune%29%2C_%22Mrs._Eleanor_Roosevelt_and_others_at_the_opening_of_Midway_Hall%2C_one_of_two_residence_halls_buil_-_NARA_-_533032.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/%28Mary_McLeod_Bethune%29%2C_%22Mrs._Eleanor_Roosevelt_and_others_at_the_opening_of_Midway_Hall%2C_one_of_two_residence_halls_buil_-_NARA_-_533032.jpg/440px-%28Mary_McLeod_Bethune%29%2C_%22Mrs._Eleanor_Roosevelt_and_others_at_the_opening_of_Midway_Hall%2C_one_of_two_residence_halls_buil_-_NARA_-_533032.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3000" data-file-height="2373" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:(Mary_McLeod_Bethune),_%22Mrs._Eleanor_Roosevelt_and_others_at_the_opening_of_Midway_Hall,_one_of_two_residence_halls_buil_-_NARA_-_533032.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Eleanor Roosevelt and <a href="/wiki/Mary_McLeod_Bethune" title="Mary McLeod Bethune">Mary McLeod Bethune</a>, a member of Roosevelt's <a href="/wiki/Black_Cabinet" title="Black Cabinet">Black Cabinet</a> (a key advisory group on race relations).</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Another significant change was establishment in 1941 of the <a href="/wiki/Fair_Employment_Practices_Committee" title="Fair Employment Practices Committee" class="mw-redirect">Fair Employment Practices Committee</a>, to implement <a href="/wiki/Executive_Order_8802" title="Executive Order 8802">Executive Order 8802</a> prohibiting racial and religious discrimination in employment among defense contractors. This was the first national program directed against employment discrimination. African Americans who gained defense industry jobs in the 1940s shared in the higher wages; in the 1950s they had gained in relative economic position, about 14% higher than other blacks who were not in such industries. Their moves into manufacturing positions was critical to their success.<sup id="cite_ref-collins_287-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-collins-287"><span>[</span>283<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Roosevelt needed the support of the powerful white Southern Democrats for his New Deal programs, and blacks were still <a href="/wiki/Disfranchisement_after_Reconstruction_era" title="Disfranchisement after Reconstruction era" class="mw-redirect">disenfranchised</a> in the South. He decided against pushing for federal anti-<a href="/wiki/Lynching" title="Lynching">lynching</a> legislation. It was not likely to pass and the political fight might threaten his ability to pass his highest priority programs—though he did denounce lynchings as "a vile form of collective murder".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoosevelt200525_288-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoosevelt200525-288"><span>[</span>284<span>]</span></a></sup> The frequency of lynchings had declined since the early decades of the century, in part due to the African Americans' Great Migration out of the South; millions were still leaving it behind.</p>
<p>Historian Kevin J. McMahon claims that strides were made for the civil rights of African Americans. In Roosevelt's Justice Department, the Civil Rights Section worked closely with the <a href="/wiki/National_Association_for_the_Advancement_of_Colored_People" title="National Association for the Advancement of Colored People">National Association for the Advancement of Colored People</a> (NAACP). Roosevelt worked with other civil rights groups on cases dealing with police brutality, lynching, and voting rights abuses.<sup id="cite_ref-289" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-289"><span>[</span>285<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Beginning in the 1960s, FDR was charged<sup id="cite_ref-290" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-290"><span>[</span>286<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-291" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-291"><span>[</span>287<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFeingold1970_292-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFeingold1970-292"><span>[</span>288<span>]</span></a></sup> with not acting decisively enough to prevent or stop <a href="/wiki/The_Holocaust" title="The Holocaust">the Holocaust</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-293" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-293"><span>[</span>289<span>]</span></a></sup> Critics cite instances such as the 1939 episode in which 936 Jewish refugees on the <a href="/wiki/SS_St._Louis" title="SS St. Louis" class="mw-redirect">SS <i>St. Louis</i></a> were denied asylum and not allowed into the United States because of strict laws passed by Congress.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFeingold197066.2C_103_294-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFeingold197066.2C_103-294"><span>[</span>290<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The issue of desegregating the armed forces did not arise, but in 1940 Roosevelt appointed former federal judge <a href="/wiki/William_H._Hastie" title="William H. Hastie">William H. Hastie</a>, an African American, to be a civilian aide to Secretary of War Henry L. Stimson.<sup id="cite_ref-295" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-295"><span>[</span>291<span>]</span></a></sup> On the home front on June 25, 1941, Roosevelt signed <a href="/wiki/Executive_Order_8802" title="Executive Order 8802">Executive Order 8802</a>, forbidding discrimination on account of "race, creed, color, or national origin" in the hiring of workers in defense related industries.<sup id="cite_ref-296" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-296"><span>[</span>292<span>]</span></a></sup> This was a precursor to Title VII of the Civil Rights Act to come decades later.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurns1970124_297-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurns1970124-297"><span>[</span>293<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Enemy aliens and people of Japanese ancestry fared badly. On February 19, 1942, Roosevelt issued <a href="/wiki/Executive_Order_9066" title="Executive Order 9066">Executive Order 9066</a> that applied to everyone classified as an "enemy alien", including people who had dual citizenship living in designated high-risk areas that covered most of the cities on the West Coast.<sup id="cite_ref-298" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-298"><span>[</span>294<span>]</span></a></sup> With the U.S. at war with Italy, some 600,000 Italian aliens (citizens of Italy who did not have U.S. citizenship) were subjected to strict travel restrictions; the restrictions were lifted in October 1942.<sup id="cite_ref-299" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-299"><span>[</span>295<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Some 120,000 people of Japanese ancestry were <a href="/wiki/Japanese_American_internment" title="Japanese American internment" class="mw-redirect">forced to leave the West Coast.</a> From 1942 to 1945, they lived in internment camps inland. Those outside the West Coast, and in <a href="/wiki/Hawaii" title="Hawaii">Hawaii</a>, were not affected.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Criticism_of_Roosevelt">Criticism of Roosevelt</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Criticism_of_Franklin_D._Roosevelt" title="Criticism of Franklin D. Roosevelt">Criticism of Franklin D. Roosevelt</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_critics_of_the_New_Deal" title="List of critics of the New Deal">List of critics of the New Deal</a></div>
<p>During his presidency, and continuing to a lesser extent today, there has been much criticism of Roosevelt, some of it intense. Critics have questioned not only <a href="/wiki/Critics_of_the_New_Deal" title="Critics of the New Deal" class="mw-redirect">his policies and positions</a>, consolidation of power that occurred due to his responses to the crises of the Depression, and World War, but also his breaking with tradition by running for a third term as president.<sup id="cite_ref-300" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-300"><span>[</span>296<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>By the middle of his second term, much criticism of Roosevelt centered on fears that he was heading toward a dictatorship, by attempting to seize control of the Supreme Court in the <a href="/wiki/Court-packing" title="Court-packing" class="mw-redirect">Court-packing</a> incident of 1937, by attempting to eliminate dissent within the Democratic party in the South during the 1938 elections, and by breaking the tradition established by <a href="/wiki/George_Washington" title="George Washington">George Washington</a> of not seeking a third term when he again ran for re-election in 1940. As two historians explain, "In 1940, with the two-term issue as a weapon, anti-New Dealers...argued that the time had come to disarm the 'dictator' and to dismantle the machinery."<sup id="cite_ref-301" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-301"><span>[</span>297<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>As President, Roosevelt was hit from both the right and the left. He came under attack for his supposed anti-business policies, for being a "warmonger", for being a "Fascist" and for being too friendly to <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Stalin" title="Joseph Stalin">Joseph Stalin</a>. Long after his death new lines of attack criticized his policies regarding helping the Jews of Europe,<sup id="cite_ref-302" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-302"><span>[</span>298<span>]</span></a></sup> incarcerating the Japanese on the West Coast,<sup id="cite_ref-303" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-303"><span>[</span>299<span>]</span></a></sup> and opposing anti-lynching legislation.<sup id="cite_ref-304" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-304"><span>[</span>300<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tmulti tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:224px;max-width:224px">
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:222px;max-width:222px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:FDR-Memorial-Grosvenor-Square.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/FDR-Memorial-Grosvenor-Square.jpg/220px-FDR-Memorial-Grosvenor-Square.jpg" width="220" height="257" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/FDR-Memorial-Grosvenor-Square.jpg/330px-FDR-Memorial-Grosvenor-Square.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/FDR-Memorial-Grosvenor-Square.jpg/440px-FDR-Memorial-Grosvenor-Square.jpg 2x" data-file-width="934" data-file-height="1091" /></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">FDR Memorial in <a href="/wiki/Grosvenor_Square" title="Grosvenor Square">Grosvenor Square</a>, London (1948)</div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:222px;max-width:222px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:FDR_Memorial_wall.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/FDR_Memorial_wall.jpg/220px-FDR_Memorial_wall.jpg" width="220" height="135" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/FDR_Memorial_wall.jpg/330px-FDR_Memorial_wall.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/FDR_Memorial_wall.jpg/440px-FDR_Memorial_wall.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1574" data-file-height="964" /></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">The Four Freedoms engraved on a wall at the <a href="/wiki/Franklin_Delano_Roosevelt_Memorial" title="Franklin Delano Roosevelt Memorial">Franklin Delano Roosevelt Memorial</a> in Washington, D.C. (1997)</div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:222px;max-width:222px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:FDR_Statue_Roosevelt_Island.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/FDR_Statue_Roosevelt_Island.JPG/220px-FDR_Statue_Roosevelt_Island.JPG" width="220" height="169" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/FDR_Statue_Roosevelt_Island.JPG/330px-FDR_Statue_Roosevelt_Island.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/FDR_Statue_Roosevelt_Island.JPG/440px-FDR_Statue_Roosevelt_Island.JPG 2x" data-file-width="4485" data-file-height="3439" /></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">FDR bust at <a href="/wiki/Franklin_D._Roosevelt_Four_Freedoms_Park" title="Franklin D. Roosevelt Four Freedoms Park">Roosevelt Island</a>, New York (2012)</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>A majority of polls rank Roosevelt as the <a href="/wiki/Historical_rankings_of_Presidents_of_the_United_States" title="Historical rankings of Presidents of the United States">second or third greatest president</a>, consistent with other surveys.<sup id="cite_ref-305" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-305"><span>[</span>301<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-306" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-306"><span>[</span>302<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-307" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-307"><span>[</span>303<span>]</span></a></sup> Roosevelt is the sixth <a href="/wiki/Gallup%27s_most_admired_man_and_woman_poll" title="Gallup's most admired man and woman poll">most admired</a> person from the 20th century by U.S. citizens, according to <a href="/wiki/Gallup_Poll" title="Gallup Poll" class="mw-redirect">Gallup</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Leuchtenburg_308-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Leuchtenburg-308"><span>[</span>304<span>]</span></a></sup> Roosevelt was also widely beloved for his role in repealing Prohibition.<sup id="cite_ref-Thornton_155-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thornton-155"><span>[</span>153<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The rapid expansion of government programs that occurred during Roosevelt's term redefined the role of the government in the United States, and Roosevelt's advocacy of government social programs was instrumental in redefining <a href="/wiki/Modern_liberalism_in_the_United_States" title="Modern liberalism in the United States">liberalism</a> for coming generations.<sup id="cite_ref-309" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-309"><span>[</span>305<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Roosevelt firmly established the United States' leadership role on the world stage, with his role in shaping and financing World War II. His isolationist critics faded away, and even the Republicans joined in his overall policies.<sup id="cite_ref-310" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-310"><span>[</span>306<span>]</span></a></sup> After his death, his widow continued to be a forceful presence in US and world politics, serving as delegate to the conference which established the United Nations and championing civil rights and liberalism generally. Many members of his administration played leading roles in the administrations of Truman, <a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy" title="John F. Kennedy">Kennedy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lyndon_B._Johnson" title="Lyndon B. Johnson">Johnson</a>, each of whom embraced Roosevelt's political legacy.<sup id="cite_ref-311" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-311"><span>[</span>307<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Reflecting on Roosevelt's presidency, "which brought the United States through the Great Depression and World War II to a prosperous future", said FDR's biographer <a href="/wiki/Jean_Edward_Smith" title="Jean Edward Smith">Jean Edward Smith</a> in 2007, "He lifted himself from a wheelchair to lift the nation from its knees."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith2007ix_312-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith2007ix-312"><span>[</span>308<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Both during and after his terms, <a href="/wiki/List_of_critics_of_the_New_Deal" title="List of critics of the New Deal">critics of Roosevelt</a> questioned not only <a href="/wiki/Criticism_of_Franklin_D._Roosevelt" title="Criticism of Franklin D. Roosevelt">his policies and positions</a>, but even more so the consolidation of power in the White House at a time when dictators were taking over Europe and Asia.<sup id="cite_ref-313" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-313"><span>[</span>309<span>]</span></a></sup> Many of the New Deal programs were abolished during the war by FDR's opponents. The powerful new wartime agencies were set up to be temporary and expire at war's end.<sup id="cite_ref-314" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-314"><span>[</span>310<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Roosevelt's <a href="/wiki/Home_of_Franklin_D._Roosevelt_National_Historic_Site" title="Home of Franklin D. Roosevelt National Historic Site">home in Hyde Park</a> is now a <a href="/wiki/National_Historic_Sites_(United_States)" title="National Historic Sites (United States)" class="mw-redirect">National Historic Site</a> and home to his <a href="/wiki/Franklin_D._Roosevelt_Presidential_Library_and_Museum" title="Franklin D. Roosevelt Presidential Library and Museum">Presidential library</a>. His <a href="/wiki/Little_White_House" title="Little White House">retreat</a> at Warm Springs, Georgia is a museum operated by the state of Georgia. His summer retreat on <a href="/wiki/Campobello_Island" title="Campobello Island">Campobello Island</a> is maintained by the governments of both Canada and the United States as <a href="/wiki/Roosevelt_Campobello_International_Park" title="Roosevelt Campobello International Park">Roosevelt Campobello International Park</a>; the island is accessible by way of the <a href="/wiki/Franklin_Delano_Roosevelt_Bridge" title="Franklin Delano Roosevelt Bridge">Franklin Delano Roosevelt Bridge</a>.</p>
<p>Washington D.C. hosts two memorials to the former president. The largest, the 7.50-acre <a href="/wiki/Franklin_Delano_Roosevelt_Memorial" title="Franklin Delano Roosevelt Memorial">Roosevelt Memorial</a>, is located next to the <a href="/wiki/Jefferson_Memorial" title="Jefferson Memorial">Jefferson Memorial</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Tidal_Basin" title="Tidal Basin">Tidal Basin</a>. A much more modest memorial, a block of marble in front of the National Archives building, was erected in 1955 and adheres to President Roosevelt's explicit suggestion as to what his memorial should be.</p>
<p>Three public sculptures of Roosevelt sitting in a wheelchair are known to exist. Two are at the Roosevelt Memorial, one of FDR sitting in a chair with small wheels&#160;– mostly obscured by his cape, another of FDR in a wheelchair at the entrance to the memorial;<sup id="cite_ref-315" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-315"><span>[</span>311<span>]</span></a></sup> a third statue, unveiled in April&#160;2008, is part of the "Paseo de los Presidentes" on the south side of <a href="/wiki/Puerto_Rico" title="Puerto Rico">Puerto Rico</a>'s Capitol Building, which honors the nine presidents who have visited the US territory while in office.<sup id="cite_ref-316" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-316"><span>[</span>312<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-317" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-317"><span>[</span>313<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-318" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-318"><span>[</span>314<span>]</span></a></sup> Another statue is installed at the <a href="/wiki/Franklin_D._Roosevelt_Four_Freedoms_Park" title="Franklin D. Roosevelt Four Freedoms Park">Franklin D. Roosevelt Four Freedoms Park</a> on <a href="/wiki/Roosevelt_Island" title="Roosevelt Island">Roosevelt Island</a>.</p>
<p>Roosevelt's leadership in the <a href="/wiki/March_of_Dimes" title="March of Dimes">March of Dimes</a> is one reason he is commemorated on the American <a href="/wiki/Dime_(United_States_coin)" title="Dime (United States coin)">dime</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-319" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-319"><span>[</span>315<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-320" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-320"><span>[</span>316<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Many parks and schools, as well as an <a href="/wiki/USS_Franklin_D._Roosevelt_(CV-42)" title="USS Franklin D. Roosevelt (CV-42)">aircraft carrier</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Franklin_D._Roosevelt_(Paris_M%C3%A9tro)" title="Franklin D. Roosevelt (Paris Métro)">Paris subway station</a> and hundreds of streets and squares both across the U.S. and the rest of the world have been named in his honor.</p>
<p>Roosevelt was a strong supporter of <a href="/wiki/Scouting" title="Scouting">scouting</a>, beginning in 1915.</p>
<p>Roosevelt was honored by the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Postal_Service" title="United States Postal Service">United States Postal Service</a> with a <a href="/wiki/Prominent_Americans_series" title="Prominent Americans series">Prominent Americans series</a> 6¢ <a href="/wiki/Postage_stamp" title="Postage stamp">postage stamp</a>, issue of 1966. He also appears on several other <a href="/wiki/U.S._Presidents_on_U.S._postage_stamps#Franklin_D._Roosevelt" title="U.S. Presidents on U.S. postage stamps" class="mw-redirect">U.S. Postage stamps</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-321" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-321"><span>[</span>317<span>]</span></a></sup> Roosevelt was a devoted <a href="/wiki/Philatelist" title="Philatelist" class="mw-redirect">philatelist</a> from the age of ten, spending some time almost every day with his collection of 1.25 million stamps. As president government agencies sent Roosevelt unusual stamps they received in the mail, and the hobby gave him an unusually thorough knowledge of world geography which benefited him during the war. After his death the collection was sold for $250,000. Among the items was a group of envelopes Roosevelt saved that he received as president; those with compliments were addressed "To the Greatest Man in the World" and "God's Gift to the U.S.A.", while less favorable letters arrived in envelopes labeled "F.D. Russianvelt, President of U.S.A., <a href="/wiki/Congress_of_Industrial_Organizations" title="Congress of Industrial Organizations">C.I.O.</a>", "<a href="/wiki/Benedict_Arnold" title="Benedict Arnold">Benedict Arnold</a> 2nd", and "Rattlesnake Roosevelt".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGunther195087.E2.80.9389.2C_337.E2.80.9338_322-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGunther195087.E2.80.9389.2C_337.E2.80.9338-322"><span>[</span>318<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The airport of the Dutch Caribbean island of <a href="/wiki/St._Eustatius" title="St. Eustatius" class="mw-redirect">St. Eustatius</a> is named <a href="/wiki/F.D._Roosevelt_Airport" title="F.D. Roosevelt Airport" class="mw-redirect">F.D. Roosevelt Airport</a> after Roosevelt, whose ancestors lived on the island in the 18th century. Most of the arms and supplies for General Washington's fight against the British came to North America through St. Eustatius. When in the port of the island in 1939, Roosevelt presented the inhabitants with a plaque commemorating that in 1776 "Here the sovereignty of the United States of America was first formally acknowledged to a national vessel by a foreign official", the famous "First Salute". The plaque hangs on the flag pole of the island's <a href="/w/index.php?title=Fort_Orange_(St._Eustatius)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Fort Orange (St. Eustatius) (page does not exist)">Fort Oranje</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Endmatter">Endmatter</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span></h3>
<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: lower-alpha;">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-7"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-7">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><i>Roosevelt</i> is an <a href="/wiki/Anglicisation" title="Anglicisation">Anglicized</a> form of the Dutch surname 'Van Rosevelt' or 'Van Rosenvelt', meaning 'from field of roses.'<sup id="cite_ref-titleRoosevelt.C2.A0.E2.80.94_Surname_Meaning_.7C_Origin_for_the_Surname_Roosevelt_Genealogy_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-titleRoosevelt.C2.A0.E2.80.94_Surname_Meaning_.7C_Origin_for_the_Surname_Roosevelt_Genealogy-6"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup> Although some use an Anglicized <a href="/wiki/Spelling_pronunciation" title="Spelling pronunciation">spelling pronunciation</a> of <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English" title="Help:IPA for English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/?/ primary stress follows">?</span><span title="'r' in 'rye'">r</span><span title="/u?/ long 'oo' in 'food'">u?</span><span title="'z' in 'Zion'">z</span><span title="/?/ 'a' in 'about'">?</span><span title="'v' in 'vie'">v</span><span title="/?/ short 'e' in 'bed'">?</span><span title="'l' in 'lie'">l</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span></span>/</a></span></span>, that is, with the vowel of <i>ruse</i>, FDR used <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[?ro?z?v?lt]</span>, with the vowel of <i>rose</i></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-11"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-11">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">It was common for boys to wear what was considered "gender-neutral" clothing, thus boys wore dresses up until they were 6 or 7.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10"><span>[</span>9<span>]</span></a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-226"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-226">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">WPA workers were counted as unemployed.<sup id="cite_ref-225" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-225"><span>[</span>223<span>]</span></a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-246"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-246">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Secretary of War Stimson did control decisions about building and the use of the atomic bomb.</span></li>
</ol>
</div>

<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Criticism">Criticism</span></h4>
<div class="refbegin" style="">
<ul>
<li><cite id="CITEREFBarnes1953" class="citation"><a href="/wiki/Harry_Elmer_Barnes" title="Harry Elmer Barnes">Barnes, Harry Elmer</a> (1953), <i>Perpetual War for Perpetual Peace: A Critical Examination of the Foreign Policy of Franklin Delano Roosevelt and Its Aftermath</i></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AFranklin+D.+Roosevelt&amp;rft.aufirst=Harry+Elmer&amp;rft.aulast=Barnes&amp;rft.btitle=Perpetual+War+for+Perpetual+Peace%3A+A+Critical+Examination+of+the+Foreign+Policy+of+Franklin+Delano+Roosevelt+and+Its+Aftermath&amp;rft.date=1953&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span>. A revisionist blames FDR for inciting Japan to attack.</li>
<li><cite id="CITEREFBest1991" class="citation">Best, Gary Dean (1991), <i>Pride, Prejudice, and Politics: Roosevelt Versus Recovery, 1933–1938</i>, Praeger</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AFranklin+D.+Roosevelt&amp;rft.aufirst=Gary+Dean&amp;rft.aulast=Best&amp;rft.btitle=Pride%2C+Prejudice%2C+and+Politics%3A+Roosevelt+Versus+Recovery%2C+1933%E2%80%931938&amp;rft.date=1991&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.pub=Praeger&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span>; summarizes newspaper editorials.</li>
<li><cite id="CITEREFBest2002" class="citation">Best, Gary Dean (2002), <i>The Retreat from Liberalism: Collectivists versus Progressives in the New Deal Years</i></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AFranklin+D.+Roosevelt&amp;rft.aufirst=Gary+Dean&amp;rft.aulast=Best&amp;rft.btitle=The+Retreat+from+Liberalism%3A+Collectivists+versus+Progressives+in+the+New+Deal+Years&amp;rft.date=2002&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span> criticizes intellectuals who supported FDR.</li>
<li><cite id="CITEREFBreitmanLichtman2013" class="citation">Breitman, Richard; <a href="/wiki/Allan_J._Lichtman" title="Allan J. Lichtman" class="mw-redirect">Lichtman, Allan J</a> (2013), <i>FDR and the Jews</i>, Harvard University Press</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AFranklin+D.+Roosevelt&amp;rft.aufirst=Richard&amp;rft.aulast=Breitman&amp;rft.au=Lichtman%2C+Allan+J&amp;rft.btitle=FDR+and+the+Jews&amp;rft.date=2013&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.pub=Harvard+University+Press&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span>, 433 pp.</li>
<li><cite id="CITEREFConkin1975" class="citation">Conkin, Paul K (1975), <i>New Deal</i></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AFranklin+D.+Roosevelt&amp;rft.aufirst=Paul+K&amp;rft.aulast=Conkin&amp;rft.btitle=New+Deal&amp;rft.date=1975&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span>, critique from the left.</li>
<li><cite id="CITEREFDoeneckeStoler2005" class="citation">Doenecke, Justus D; Stoler, Mark A (2005), <i>Debating Franklin D. Roosevelt's Foreign Policies, 1933–1945</i>, Rowman &amp; Littlefield</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AFranklin+D.+Roosevelt&amp;rft.aufirst=Justus+D&amp;rft.aulast=Doenecke&amp;rft.au=Stoler%2C+Mark+A&amp;rft.btitle=Debating+Franklin+D.+Roosevelt%27s+Foreign+Policies%2C+1933%E2%80%931945&amp;rft.date=2005&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.pub=Rowman+%26+Littlefield&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span>. 248 pp.</li>
<li><cite id="CITEREFFeingold1970" class="citation">Feingold, Henry L (1970), <i>The Politics of Rescue: The Roosevelt Administration and the Holocaust, 1938–1945</i></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AFranklin+D.+Roosevelt&amp;rft.aufirst=Henry+L&amp;rft.aulast=Feingold&amp;rft.btitle=The+Politics+of+Rescue%3A+The+Roosevelt+Administration+and+the+Holocaust%2C+1938%E2%80%931945&amp;rft.date=1970&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span>.</li>
<li><cite id="CITEREFFlynn1948" class="citation"><a href="/wiki/John_T._Flynn" title="John T. Flynn">Flynn, John T</a> (1948), <i>The Roosevelt Myth</i></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AFranklin+D.+Roosevelt&amp;rft.aufirst=John+T&amp;rft.aulast=Flynn&amp;rft.btitle=The+Roosevelt+Myth&amp;rft.date=1948&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span>, former FDR supporter condemns all aspects of FDR.</li>
<li><cite id="CITEREFMoley1939" class="citation"><a href="/wiki/Raymond_Moley" title="Raymond Moley">Moley, Raymond</a> (1939), <i>After Seven Years</i> (insider memoir by Brain Truster who became conservative)</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AFranklin+D.+Roosevelt&amp;rft.aufirst=Raymond&amp;rft.aulast=Moley&amp;rft.btitle=After+Seven+Years&amp;rft.date=1939&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span>.</li>
<li><cite id="CITEREFRussett1997" class="citation"><a href="/wiki/Bruce_Russett" title="Bruce Russett">Russett, Bruce M</a> (1997), <i>No Clear and Present Danger: A Skeptical View of the United States Entry into World War II</i> (2nd ed.)</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AFranklin+D.+Roosevelt&amp;rft.aufirst=Bruce+M&amp;rft.aulast=Russett&amp;rft.btitle=No+Clear+and+Present+Danger%3A+A+Skeptical+View+of+the+United+States+Entry+into+World+War+II&amp;rft.date=1997&amp;rft.edition=2nd&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span>, says US should have let USSR and Germany destroy each other.</li>

<li><cite id="CITEREFRobinson2001" class="citation">Robinson, Greg (2001), <i>By Order of the President: FDR and the Internment of Japanese Americans</i></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AFranklin+D.+Roosevelt&amp;rft.aufirst=Greg&amp;rft.aulast=Robinson&amp;rft.btitle=By+Order+of+the+President%3A+FDR+and+the+Internment+of+Japanese+Americans&amp;rft.date=2001&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span> says FDR's racism was primarily to blame.</li>
<li><cite id="CITEREFSchivelbusch2006" class="citation"><a href="/wiki/Wolfgang_Schivelbusch" title="Wolfgang Schivelbusch">Schivelbusch, Wolfgang</a> (2006), <i>Three New Deals: Reflections on Roosevelt's America, Mussolini's Italy, and Hitler's Germany, 1933–1939</i></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AFranklin+D.+Roosevelt&amp;rft.aufirst=Wolfgang&amp;rft.aulast=Schivelbusch&amp;rft.btitle=Three+New+Deals%3A+Reflections+on+Roosevelt%27s+America%2C+Mussolini%27s+Italy%2C+and+Hitler%27s+Germany%2C+1933%E2%80%931939&amp;rft.date=2006&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span>, compares populist and paternalist features.</li>
<li><cite id="CITEREFSmiley1993" class="citation">Smiley, Gene (1993), <i>Rethinking the Great Depression</i> (short essay)</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AFranklin+D.+Roosevelt&amp;rft.aufirst=Gene&amp;rft.aulast=Smiley&amp;rft.btitle=Rethinking+the+Great+Depression&amp;rft.date=1993&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span> by <a href="/wiki/Libertarianism" title="Libertarianism">libertarian</a> economist who blames both Hoover and FDR.</li>
<li><cite id="CITEREFWyman1984" class="citation"><a href="/wiki/David_Wyman" title="David Wyman">Wyman, David S</a> (1984), <i><a href="/wiki/The_Abandonment_of_the_Jews:_America_and_the_Holocaust_1941%E2%80%931945" title="The Abandonment of the Jews: America and the Holocaust 1941–1945" class="mw-redirect">The Abandonment of the Jews: America and the Holocaust 1941–1945</a></i>, Pantheon Books</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AFranklin+D.+Roosevelt&amp;rft.aufirst=David+S&amp;rft.aulast=Wyman&amp;rft.btitle=The+Abandonment+of+the+Jews%3A+America+and+the+Holocaust+1941%E2%80%931945&amp;rft.date=1984&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.pub=Pantheon+Books&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span>. Attacks Roosevelt for passive complicity in allowing Holocaust to happen.</li>

<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Primary_sources">Primary sources</span></h4>
<div class="refbegin" style="">
<ul>
<li><cite id="CITEREF1951" class="citation"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www2.census.gov/prod2/statcomp/documents/1946-01.pdf"><i>Statistical Abstract of the United States</i></a> <span style="font-size:85%;">(PDF)</span>, Bureau of the Census, 1951</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AFranklin+D.+Roosevelt&amp;rft.btitle=Statistical+Abstract+of+the+United+States&amp;rft.date=1951&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww2.census.gov%2Fprod2%2Fstatcomp%2Fdocuments%2F1946-01.pdf&amp;rft.pub=Bureau+of+the+Census&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span>; full of useful data</li>
<li><cite id="CITEREF1976" class="citation"><i>Historical Statistics of the United States: Colonial Times to 1970</i>, Bureau of the Census, 1976</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AFranklin+D.+Roosevelt&amp;rft.btitle=Historical+Statistics+of+the+United+States%3A+Colonial+Times+to+1970&amp;rft.date=1976&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.pub=Bureau+of+the+Census&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span>.</li>
<li><cite id="CITEREFCantrilStrunk1951" class="citation">Cantril, Hadley; Strunk, Mildred, eds. (1951), <i>Public Opinion, 1935–1946</i></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AFranklin+D.+Roosevelt&amp;rft.btitle=Public+Opinion%2C+1935%E2%80%931946&amp;rft.date=1951&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span>, massive compilation of many public opinion polls from the USA.</li>
<li><cite id="CITEREFGallup1972" class="citation">Gallup, George Horace, ed. (1972), <i>The Gallup Poll; Public Opinion, 1935–1971</i></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AFranklin+D.+Roosevelt&amp;rft.btitle=The+Gallup+Poll%3B+Public+Opinion%2C+1935%E2%80%931971&amp;rft.date=1972&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span>, 3 vol, summarizes results of each poll as reported to newspapers.</li>
<li><cite id="CITEREFLoewenheimLangley1975" class="citation">Loewenheim, Francis L; <a href="/wiki/Harold_D._Langley" title="Harold D. Langley">Langley, Harold D</a>, eds. (1975), <i>Roosevelt and Churchill: Their Secret Wartime Correspondence</i></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AFranklin+D.+Roosevelt&amp;rft.btitle=Roosevelt+and+Churchill%3A+Their+Secret+Wartime+Correspondence&amp;rft.date=1975&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span>.</li>
<li><cite id="CITEREFMoley1939" class="citation">Moley, Raymond (1939), <i>After Seven Years</i> (memoir)</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AFranklin+D.+Roosevelt&amp;rft.aufirst=Raymond&amp;rft.aulast=Moley&amp;rft.btitle=After+Seven+Years&amp;rft.date=1939&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span> by key Brain Truster</li>
<li><cite id="CITEREFNixon1969" class="citation">Nixon, Edgar B, ed. (1969), <i>Franklin D Roosevelt and Foreign Affairs</i></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AFranklin+D.+Roosevelt&amp;rft.btitle=Franklin+D+Roosevelt+and+Foreign+Affairs&amp;rft.date=1969&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span> (3 vol), covers 1933–37. 2nd series 1937–39 available on microfiche and in a 14 vol print edition at some academic libraries.</li>
<li><cite id="CITEREFRoosevelt1945" class="citation">Roosevelt, Franklin Delano (1945) [1938], Rosenman, Samuel Irving, ed., <i>The Public Papers and Addresses of Franklin D. Roosevelt</i> (public material only (no letters); covers 1928–1945), 13 volumes</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AFranklin+D.+Roosevelt&amp;rft.aufirst=Franklin+Delano&amp;rft.aulast=Roosevelt&amp;rft.btitle=The+Public+Papers+and+Addresses+of+Franklin+D.+Roosevelt&amp;rft.date=1945&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.volume=13+volumes" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span>.</li>
<li><cite id="CITEREFRoosevelt1946" class="citation">——— (1946), Zevin, BD, ed., <i>Nothing to Fear: The Selected Addresses of Franklin Delano Roosevelt, 1932–1945</i> (selected speeches)</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AFranklin+D.+Roosevelt&amp;rft.aufirst=Franklin+Delano&amp;rft.aulast=Roosevelt&amp;rft.btitle=Nothing+to+Fear%3A+The+Selected+Addresses+of+Franklin+Delano+Roosevelt%2C+1932%E2%80%931945&amp;rft.date=1946&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span>.</li>
<li><cite id="CITEREFRoosevelt2005" class="citation">——— (2005) [1947], Taylor, Myron C, ed., <i>Wartime Correspondence Between President Roosevelt and Pope Pius XII</i> (reprint), Prefaces by <a href="/wiki/Pius_XII" title="Pius XII" class="mw-redirect">Pius XII</a> and <a href="/wiki/Harry_Truman" title="Harry Truman" class="mw-redirect">Harry Truman</a>, Kessinger Publishing, <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/1-4191-6654-9" title="Special:BookSources/1-4191-6654-9">1-4191-6654-9</a></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AFranklin+D.+Roosevelt&amp;rft.aufirst=Franklin+Delano&amp;rft.aulast=Roosevelt&amp;rft.btitle=Wartime+Correspondence+Between+President+Roosevelt+and+Pope+Pius+XII&amp;rft.date=2005&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.isbn=1-4191-6654-9&amp;rft.pub=Kessinger+Publishing&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span>.</li>
<li><cite class="citation"><i>Documentary History of the Franklin D. Roosevelt Administration</i></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AFranklin+D.+Roosevelt&amp;rft.btitle=Documentary+History+of+the+Franklin+D.+Roosevelt+Administration&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span>, 20 vol. available in some large academic libraries.</li>
